Incarnation and Union: Disbelief and Interpretation
Warns against incarnation and union, declares them disbelief, and clarifies Sufi metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in incarnation (hulul) and union (ittihad) is disbelief, and that any ambiguous statements from Sufis must be interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
Incarnation and union are disbelief; even ignorance of that is disbelief.

Having established the impossibility of union, the author now warns that believing in such doctrines is outright disbelief. He also addresses the Batiniyya and Christian claims, and clarifies that certain Sufi expressions that might seem to suggest union are metaphorical and must be interpreted accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on disbelief
Read the Arabic passage from 'ومثل الحلول' to 'كفر كفر'. Notice how the author connects incarnation and union as equally impossible and as disbelief.
The passage includes a discussion on metaphorical language from a previous verse.
2
Understand the metaphorical distinction
The author quotes al-Ida'a to explain the difference between worship and enmity. The 'lam' (letter lam) in both cases is metaphorical, but the direction of metaphor differs. Analogies to other cases can be drawn.
This part may seem digressive, but it shows how language can be misinterpreted.
3
Identify the warning against Christians and Batiniyya
The author explicitly warns not to listen to Christian doctrines of incarnation or the claims of the Batiniyya, calling them cults of deviation and atheism.
These groups are considered outside the fold of Islam for these beliefs.
4
Interpret Sufi statements
The author notes that some eminent Sufis have said things that might imply union, but these are to be interpreted according to their conventional usage and the clear texts of revelation.
This protects against misreading Sufi poetry and ecstatic utterances.

Source Text

God's Independence from a Specifier and His Oneness
Asserts that God needs no specifier and must be one in essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot need a specifier and what the three aspects of His oneness are.

Core Idea
God is independent and one in essence, attributes, and actions with no partner.

Sanusi now establishes two fundamental divine attributes: God's independence from any external cause and His absolute oneness. These are necessary implications of His perfection and eternity. The passage first refutes the idea that God could need a specifier, then unfolds the threefold meaning of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: first the negation of a specifier, then the affirmation of oneness with three negations.
The passage uses the pattern 'mustahil an...' for impossibilities.
2
Analyze the first argument: independence
Understand why needing a specifier implies temporality (huduth), which is impossible for God. This establishes God's independence (ghina).
3
Examine the three aspects of oneness
Identify the three negations: no composition in essence, no likeness in essence or attributes, no partner effecter in any action. Each negation guards a specific false belief.
These correspond to oneness of essence, attributes, and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يفتقر إلى مخَصِّص لأنه لو احتاج إلى مخصص حادثا والحدوث على الله محال. ويستحيل أن لا يكون واحداً : فيجب أن يكون الله واحداً لا مركباً في ذاته، أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته أو في صفاته أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال. فيستحيل
And it is impossible for Allah to need a specifier, because if He needed a specifier, He would be temporal, and temporality is impossible for Allah. And it is impossible for Him not to be one: so it is necessary that Allah be one, not composite in His essence, nor having any likeness in His essence or attributes, nor having with Him in existence an effecter in any action from actions. So it is impossible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage covers two necessary divine attributes: independence (ghina) and oneness (tawhid). The first: God cannot need a specifier (مخصص) because needing a specifier would entail temporal origination (حدوث), which is impossible for the eternal God. The second: God must be one (واحد) in three ways: (1) not composite (مركب) in essence, meaning His essence is not made of parts; (2) no likeness (مماثل) to Him in essence or attributes, meaning He is unique and nothing resembles Him; (3) no other effecter (مؤثر) exists with Him in causing any action, meaning He alone is the creator of all actions. The author concludes that these negations are necessary.

Key Terms
مخصص
حدوث
واحد
مركب
مماثل
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's oneness only means numerical singleness.
Correction: The passage shows oneness includes no composition in essence, no likeness, and no partner in causation.

Checkpoint
What are the three aspects of oneness mentioned in the passage?
A) Oneness of essence, attributes, and actions.
B) Not composite, no likeness, no partner in causing any action.
C) Unity of command, unity of worship, unity of lordship.
D) Oneness in number, oneness in eternity, oneness in power.

Key Takeaway
God is necessarily independent and one in three ways: no composition, no likeness, no partner in action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage states God is not composite, has no likeness, and no partner in action. Which of these aspects of oneness do you most struggle to live by? Do you find yourself attributing power or worth to created things as if they share God's attributes? What is one concrete step you can take this week to purify your acknowledgment of God's sole agency and uniqueness?

Reward, Punishment, and Vision as Permissible
Enumeration of three permissible matters with special focus on vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reward, punishment, and the vision of God are rationally permissible but their actual occurrence is known only through revelation.

Core Idea
What is rationally possible is not always actual until revelation informs us.

The author now adds specific outcomes—reward, punishment, and seeing God—to the category of the permissible. These are things that could happen, but we only know they will happen because Allah has told us. The vision of God is particularly important: it will occur in the afterlife, but in this world it is prohibited by revelation, even though it is not rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage first without looking at the translation. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Pay attention to how the author distinguishes rational permissibility from actual occurrence.
2
Identify the three items listed as permissible
Find the three things the author includes under the permissible: reward of the obedient, punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God.
These are all matters of the unseen that reason cannot mandate.
3
Understand the ruling on vision in this world vs. the afterlife
Note that vision in the afterlife is both permissible and actual, while in this world it is permissible rationally but prohibited by revelation. The author cites a hadith and the consensus of verifiers.
This is a key nuance.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
Realize that reason alone cannot tell us whether these events will occur; we depend on revelation to know their actuality.
This reinforces the sam'iyyat category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل في الجائز ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي، ورؤية الله تعالى فيراه المؤمنون منزها عن الجهة والمقابلة والمكان وغير ذلك من الأمور العادية، فكما علموه منزها يرونه كذلك، فرؤيته تعالى جائزة وواقعة في الآخرة للمؤمنين وأما في الدنيا فجائزة عقلاً ممنوعة شرعاً الحديث لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا، وأما في الدنيا فلم تقع لأحد كما هو المعتمد عند المحققين. فكل هذا جائز في العقل لكن الشرع أخبر بوقوعها فيجب الإيمان به. (۱) ونازع البعض فادعى وقوعها للنبي عليه السلام، وهو مرجوح، والخلاف فيها يسير.
And included in the permissible is the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient, and the vision of God, may He be Exalted. The believers will see Him, transcending direction, facing, place, and other ordinary matters. So as they knew Him to be transcendent, they see Him likewise. So His vision, may He be Exalted, is permissible and occurs in the afterlife for the believers. As for in this world, it is rationally permissible but prohibited by revelation: the hadith 'You will not see your Lord until you die.' As for in this world, it has not occurred for anyone, as is the relied-upon view among the verifiers. So all of this is permissible in the intellect, but revelation has informed of its occurrence, so faith in it is obligatory. (1) Some disputed and claimed its occurrence for the Prophet, peace be upon him, but that is outweighed, and the disagreement about it is minor.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage adds reward, punishment, and the vision of God to the category of permissible (ja'iz). It emphasizes that the vision of God in the afterlife is real but occurs without direction, place, or material conditions. In this world, vision is rationally possible but forbidden by revelation, citing the hadith 'You will not see your Lord until you die.' The author notes that the preferred view is that no one, including the Prophet, has seen God in this world, though some dissent is acknowledged. The key point is that these matters are rationally permissible but their actual occurrence is confirmed by revelation, obligating belief.

Key Terms
ثواب المطيع
عقاب العاصي
رؤية الله
الجهة
المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because vision of God is rationally possible, it occurs in this world or that the Prophet saw God in this world.
Correction: The passage clarifies that vision in this world is prohibited by revelation and has not occurred for anyone, including the Prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on seeing God in this world?
A) Rationally impossible
B) Rationally permissible but prohibited by revelation
C) Obligatory
D) Occurred for the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Reward, punishment, and vision of God are rationally permissible; their actual occurrence is known only through revelation and must be believed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Proof of God's Existence: The World's Temporality
Sanusi opens the proofs section and defines the proof of God's existence via the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proof of God's existence in classical kalam is the temporality of the world, and why establishing proofs is necessary to move beyond blind following.

Core Idea
The world's coming into existence is itself the proof of God.

This passage marks a turning point in the text. Having laid out the rational judgments about God without proof, Sanusi now introduces the key term 'proof' (burhān) and presents the foundational proof for God's existence: the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). This short but dense section defines both terms and explains why proofs are essential for sound belief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Note that the preceding sections only explained the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) regarding God without providing proofs. Sanusi now begins the proofs section.
The transition from definitions to proofs is crucial for moving from taqlid to certainty.
2
Grasp the problem of taqlid
Sanusi mentions that accepting beliefs without proof makes one a muqallid (blind follower), and there is scholarly disagreement about the ruling of such a person. Providing proofs resolves this disagreement.
This highlights the importance of rational demonstration in Islamic theology.
3
Read the proof statement carefully
The proof of God's existence is explicitly defined as the temporality of the world: 'burhān wujūd Allāh huwa ḥudūth al-ʿālam.' Read this equation directly from the Arabic.
This is not a separate argument; the temporality itself is the proof.
4
Analyze the definitions
Sanusi defines temporality (ḥudūth) as existence after non-existence, and the world (al-ʿālam) as every existent other than God. The emergence of the world from non-existence to existence is the evidence.
These precise definitions are essential for the logical proof that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشروع في البراهين على العقائد واعلم أن ما مرَّ كان عبارة عن بيان الأقسام الثلاثة ما يجب وما يستحيل وما يجوز في حق الله تعالى من دون ذكر للأدلة، وهذا إذا قبله الإنسان واعتقد به فإنه مؤسس ولكنه مقلد ، وقد مرَّ الخلاف في حكم المقلد ، وللخروج من الخلاف سنذكر هنا فيما يلي الأدلة العقلية على ما مضى. برهان وجود الله هو حدوث العالم، والمراد بالحدوث الوجود بعد العدم، والمراد بالعالم كل موجود سوى الله تعالى، أي إن خروج العالم من العدم إلى الوجود هو دليل
Commencing the proofs upon the beliefs. And know that what has passed was an explanation of the three divisions: what is necessary, what is impossible, and what is possible with respect to God, exalted, without mentioning proofs. If a person accepts this and believes it, then he is founded, but he is a muqallid (blind follower). The disagreement concerning the ruling of the muqallid has passed. To exit from the disagreement, we will mention here, in what follows, the rational proofs (al-adilla al-ʿaqliyya) for what has preceded. The proof of the existence of God (burhān wujūd Allāh) is the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). By temporality is meant existence after non-existence, and by the world is meant every existent other than God, exalted. That is, the emergence of the world from non-existence to existence is the proof.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the proofs section (al-barāhīn) in the text. The author transitions from the previous discussion of the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) regarding God, which were presented without proofs, to now providing rational proofs for those beliefs. He notes that accepting those judgments without proof makes one a muqallid (blind follower), and there is scholarly disagreement on the ruling of such a person. To resolve that disagreement, he will present the rational proofs. He then states the first proof: the proof of God's existence (burhān wujūd Allāh) is the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). He defines temporality as existence after non-existence (al-wujūd baʿd al-ʿadam) and defines the world as every existent other than God. Thus, the very fact that the world came into existence from non-existence serves as evidence for God's existence. This is a concise statement of the cosmological argument from contingency.

Correction: The text explicitly equates the proof with the temporality: 'The proof of the existence of God is the temporality of the world.' The world's coming into existence from non-existence is itself the evidence.

Key Takeaway

The Proof of God's Existence: Definitions
Sanusi defines huduth and alam and presents the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of huduth (occurrence) and alam (world) and how they form the foundational proof of God's existence.

Core Idea
The world's coming into existence after nothing proves its Maker.

Sanusi begins the proof of God's existence by clarifying two key terms: huduth and alam. Understanding these definitions is essential because the entire argument hinges on the world's status as an originated product. In this lesson, you will see how the simple observation of the world's beginning leads directly to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi first defines two terms before stating the proof.
The definitions are precise; pay attention to 'existence after nonexistence'.
2
Understand the definitions
Break down the definitions of huduth (existence after nonexistence) and alam (every existent other than Allah). Why is this distinction important?
3
Grasp the logical chain
The world is hadith (originated), meaning it is a product (sina'ah). Every product must have a maker. Therefore, the world has a maker, whose name is Allah according to the prophets.
The proof is not the world's existence per se, but its origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجود الله هو حدوث العالم، والمراد بالحدوث الوجود بعد العدم، والمراد بالعالم كل موجود سوى الله تعالى، أي إن خروج العالم من العدم إلى الوجود هو دليل على وجود الله تعالى، لأن العالم في هذه الحالة يكون حادثاً أي صنعة والصنعة لا بد لها من صانع وهو الذي ورد عن الأنبياء أن اسمه "الله"
The proof of the existence of God is the occurrence (huduth) of the world. By 'occurrence' is meant existence after nonexistence, and by 'world' is meant every existent other than God Almighty. That is, the world's coming into existence from nonexistence is evidence for the existence of God Almighty, because the world in this state is originated (hadith), i.e., a product, and a product necessarily has a maker, and it is the one that has come from the prophets that His name is 'Allah'.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines the proof of God's existence. It first defines 'occurrence' (huduth) as existence after nonexistence. Then it defines 'world' (alam) as everything that exists other than God. The proof itself is the world's transition from nonexistence to existence, which indicates a maker. Since the world is originated, it is a product (sina'ah), and every product must have a maker (sani'). That maker is identified as 'Allah' according to prophetic tradition.

Key Terms

عالم
حادث
صنعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the world's existence itself, without considering its origination, is the proof.
Correction: The proof specifically rests on the world's having come into existence after nonexistence, not just its existence.

Checkpoint

A) The world's eternal existence.
B) Existence after nonexistence.
C) The world's complexity.
D) The world's perfection.

Key Takeaway
The world's origination from nonexistence proves the existence of a Creator, known as Allah.

Possibility and Existence in Proving God
Sanusi clarifies the role of existence in the contingency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere possibility of the world is insufficient to prove God's existence, and why the actual existence of contingent beings is the key evidence.

Core Idea
Possibility signals need; existence proves the Giver.

This lesson refines the classical Kalam argument for God's existence. While the contingency of the world indicates its need for a cause, it is the sheer fact that contingent things exist—rather than merely being possible—that drives the proof. We read Sanusi's clarification to grasp this nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation to understand the core claim.
Notice how the author distinguishes between 'imkan' (possibility) and 'wujud' (existence).
2
Distinguish possibility from existence
Reflect on why possibility alone is not a proof; possibility before existence only shows potential need, not actual need for a preponderator.
3
Understand why existence necessitates a preponderator
The existence of a possible being forces the mind to seek a cause that gave it existence, i.e., a preponderator (murajjiḥ).
This is the core of the argument from contingency.
4
Consider the strengthening condition
The passage mentions that if we also prove the world is originated (not eternal), the argument becomes stronger. Think about why that is.
Proving origination removes the possibility of an eternal contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنّ الإمكان ثابت للعالم قبل وجوده ، فلا يجوز أن يكون مطلق دليلاً على وجود الله تعالى، بل وجود الممكن هو الدليل للاضطرار إلى المرجّح ، ويصبح الاستدلال بوجود الممكن أقوى إذا أثبتنا لزوم حدوثه و امتناع قدمه، فيلزم حينذاك ضرورة الاحتياج إلى واجب للوجود ، ومن خصائص واجب الوجود القدم ، فالإمكان وحده دليل الاحتياج الثابت للممكن ، ووجود الممكن دليل وجود الله تعالى عند نظر الإنسان فيه (س) .
And know that possibility is established for the world before its existence, so it is not permissible for it to be an absolute proof for the existence of Allah. Rather, the existence of the possible is the proof because it necessitates the preponderator. And the inference becomes stronger if we prove its origination and the impossibility of its pre-eternity. Then the necessity of need for the Necessary Existent follows, and among the characteristics of the Necessary Existent is pre-eternity. So possibility alone is proof of the need established for the possible, and the existence of the possible is proof of the existence of Allah when man reflects upon it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the role of contingency (imkān) in the argument for God's existence. It distinguishes between possibility itself and the actual existence of a possible being. Possibility (imkān) indicates that a thing is not necessary and therefore in need of a cause, but it is the actual existence of the possible that proves the existence of a preponderator (murajjiḥ) who brought it into existence. The author notes that the argument becomes stronger if we can prove that the possible world is originated (ḥādith) and not eternal, because then the necessity of a pre-existent Necessary Existent is more evident.

Key Terms
إمكان
ممكن
واجب الوجود
مرجح
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving the world merely possible is enough to prove God.
Correction: This passage emphasizes that the existence of the possible is the actual trigger for the inference; possibility alone only shows potential need.

Checkpoint
What does the existence of a possible being (mumkin) prove?
A) That possibility itself is necessary.
B) That the possible is eternal.
C) That there must be a preponderator.
D) That the possible is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Possibility indicates need, but it is the existence of contingent things that actively proves the existence of a necessary preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the existence of the world around you, do you truly recognize the 'preponderator' that has brought it into being? What would change in your daily life if you constantly saw every existing thing as a direct sign of God's existence?
?
The passage says that existence of the possible is proof of Allah 'when man reflects upon it.' How much genuine reflection do you give to the existence of things? Are you content to let the universe's existence be an abstract fact without letting it drive you to conviction?
?
Your own existence is a concrete proof of God. Do you live as if that is true, or do you treat your life as a mere accident? What would it look like to respond to this proof with gratitude and submission?

The Division of the World into Substances and Accidents
Sanusi defines the world's two parts and states the proof method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of substances and accidents and the overall method of proving the world's origination.

Core Idea
The world consists of only two categories: substances and accidents.

Sanusi begins by establishing the basic ontological division of the observable world. Before proving that the world is created, we must agree on what it contains. This passage defines substances as self-subsistent entities and accidents as their qualities, then outlines the strategy to prove each category is originated.

Guided Path
1
Define substances and accidents
Read the definitions carefully. Note that substances (ajrām) are what stand by themselves, while accidents (aʿrāḍ) are what exist in substances.
2
Understand the proof strategy
The author states that proving the world's origination requires proving each division is originated. This sets up the next lesson.
3
Reflect on the completeness of the division
Consider why the world must be entirely composed of these two categories. Could there be something else? The passage asserts there is nothing beyond them.
This division is fundamental to kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم ينقسم بالنظر والمشاهدة إلى الأجرام وهي ما يقوم بذاته كالأجسام، والأعراض وهي ما يقوم بالأجرام كالحركة والسكون والألوان والكيفيات المختلفة وغير ذلك، فطريقة البرهان على حدوث العالم أن يتم البرهان على حدوث كل من القسمين وبهذا يتم البرهان على حدوث العالم، لأنه ليس شيئا غيرهما. فدليل حدوث الأجرام، أن كل جرم فهو ملازم للأعراض الحادثة المتجددة من حركة وسكون واجتماع وافتراق وهذه الأعراض الأربعة تسمى الأكوان الأربعة، والجرم لا يخلو من أحدها ، لأنه إما أن يكون متحركاً أو ساكناً أو مجتمعاً أو مفترقاً، ومن الأعراض الطعوم والروائح والألوان وغير ذلك، والأعراض حادثة ودليله تغيرها كما يأتي، والجرم الملازم لها حادث مثلها لملازمته لها. (1) يخص العلماء الحركة والسكون بالكلام عليهما في أثناء بيان حدوث العالم، لأن التحقيق أن أغلب الأعراض ترجع إليهما (س). (2) ما لازم الحادث لا بد أن يكون حادثاً، وهذه المقدمة قوية جداً، فإنه قد ثبت أن الجرم لا ينفك عن الأعراض بالمشاهدة والدليل العقلي، وثبت أن الأعراض كلها حادثة أي لجميع الأعراض أول وبداية لم تكن موجودة قبلها، وبما أن الجرم يستحيل وجوده بدون الأعراض، فصار كأن وجوده مشروط بوجود الأعراض، ولما كانت الأعراض حادثة، صار وجود الجرم مشروطاً بحادث ، ما كان مشروطة وجوده بحادث فلا بد أن يكون حادثاً بهذا يثبت حدوث الأجرام. واعلم أن ابن تيمية
The world is divided, by observation and sight, into substances (ajrām) which are what subsist by themselves like bodies, and accidents (aʿrāḍ) which are what subsist in substances like motion, rest, colors, various qualities, and other things. So the method of proof for the origination of the world is to prove the origination of each of the two divisions, and thereby the origination of the world is proven, because there is nothing other than them. The proof of the origination of substances: every substance is inseparable from originating, renewing accidents, from motion, rest, conjunction, and separation, and these four accidents are called the four 'akwān. The substance cannot be free from one of them, because it is either moving or at rest or joined or separated. Among the accidents are tastes, smells, colors, and others. Accidents are originated, and their proof is their change, as will come. And the substance that is inseparable from them is originated like them because of its inseparability from them. (1) The scholars specify motion and rest for discussion during the explanation of the origination of the world, because the reality is that most accidents reduce to them. (2) Whatever is inseparable from an originated thing must itself be originated. This premise is very strong. For it has been established that the substance is not free from accidents by observation and rational proof, and it has been established that all accidents are originated, i.e., all accidents have a first beginning that did not exist before. And since the substance's existence is impossible without accidents, it becomes as if its existence is conditioned by the existence of accidents. And when accidents are originated, the existence of the substance becomes conditioned by an originated thing. Whatever is conditioned in its existence by an originated thing must itself be originated. By this, the origination of substances is proven. And know that Ibn Taymiyya...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) by dividing it into substances and accidents. It first defines substances (ajrām/ajsām) as self-subsistent entities and accidents (aʿrāḍ) as attributes inhering in substances. The proof then focuses on substances: every substance is always inseparable from accidents, especially the four primary accidents (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) known as al-akwān al-arbaʿa. Since these accidents are observed to change (hence originated), the substance that cannot exist without them must also be originated. The footnotes explain that motion and rest are the most important accidents, and they provide a strong logical principle: whatever is inseparable from an originated thing must itself be originated (mā lāzama al-ḥādith fa-lā budda an yakūna ḥādithan). This is a foundational argument for the origination of the material world.

Key Terms
أجرام
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that accidents are independent entities like substances.
Correction: Accidents are defined as what subsist in substances; they have no independent existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two divisions of the world?
A) Substances and accidents
B) Bodies and souls
C) Matter and form
D) Atoms and voids

Key Takeaway
The world is exhaustively composed of substances and accidents; proving its origination requires proving each is created.

Proof that Substances are Originated
Sanusi presents the proof for the origination of substances via the principle of inseparability from originated accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that all substances are originated because they are inseparable from originated accidents.

Core Idea
Whatever is inseparable from an originated thing must itself be originated.

Having defined the two divisions, Sanusi now proves that substances are created. The key premise is that every substance is always accompanied by changing accidents, particularly the four primary accidents of motion, rest, conjunction, and separation. Since these accidents are observed to change, they must have a beginning, and the substance that cannot exist without them must likewise have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four primary accidents
The passage lists motion, rest, conjunction, and separation as the accidents that substances never lack. These are called the 'four akwān'.
2
Understand the logical principle
The principle 'whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated' is the core. The passage explains it clearly.
3
Apply the principle to substances
Since substances are inseparable from these originated accidents, they must be originated as well.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
The footnote emphasizes that this premise is very strong and rationally justified.
This is a foundational kalam proof for the creation of the material world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(1) يخص العلماء الحركة والسكون بالكلام عليهما في أثناء بيان حدوث العالم، لأن التحقيق أن أغلب الأعراض ترجع إليهما (س). (2) ما لازم الحادث لا بد أن يكون حادثاً، وهذه المقدمة قوية جداً، فإنه قد ثبت أن الجرم لا ينفك عن الأعراض بالمشاهدة والدليل العقلي، وثبت أن الأعراض كلها حادثة أي لجميع الأعراض أول وبداية لم تكن موجودة قبلها، وبما أن الجرم يستحيل وجوده بدون الأعراض، فصار كأن وجوده مشروط بوجود الأعراض، ولما كانت الأعراض حادثة، صار وجود الجرم مشروطاً بحادث ، ما كان مشروطة وجوده بحادث فلا بد أن يكون حادثاً بهذا يثبت حدوث الأجرام. واعلم أن ابن تيمية
The world is divided, by observation and sight, into substances (ajrām) which are what subsist by themselves like bodies, and accidents (aʿrāḍ) which are what subsist in substances like motion, rest, colors, various qualities, and other things. So the method of proof for the origination of the world is to prove the origination of each of the two divisions, and thereby the origination of the world is proven, because there is nothing other than them. The proof of the origination of substances: every substance is inseparable from originating, renewing accidents, from motion, rest, conjunction, and separation, and these four accidents are called the four 'akwān. The substance cannot be free from one of them, because it is either moving or at rest or joined or separated. Among the accidents are tastes, smells, colors, and others. Accidents are originated, and their proof is their change, as will come. And the substance that is inseparable from them is originated like them because of its inseparability from them. (1) The scholars specify motion and rest for discussion during the explanation of the origination of the world, because the reality is that most accidents reduce to them. (2) Whatever is inseparable from an originated thing must itself be originated. This premise is very strong. For it has been established that the substance is not free from accidents by observation and rational proof, and it has been established that all accidents are originated, i.e., all accidents have a first beginning that did not exist before. And since the substance's existence is impossible without accidents, it becomes as if its existence is conditioned by the existence of accidents. And when accidents are originated, the existence of the substance becomes conditioned by an originated thing. Whatever is conditioned in its existence by an originated thing must itself be originated. By this, the origination of substances is proven. And know that Ibn Taymiyya...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) by dividing it into substances and accidents. It first defines substances (ajrām/ajsām) as self-subsistent entities and accidents (aʿrāḍ) as attributes inhering in substances. The proof then focuses on substances: every substance is always inseparable from accidents, especially the four primary accidents (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) known as al-akwān al-arbaʿa. Since these accidents are observed to change (hence originated), the substance that cannot exist without them must also be originated. The footnotes explain that motion and rest are the most important accidents, and they provide a strong logical principle: whatever is inseparable from an originated thing must itself be originated (mā lāzama al-ḥādith fa-lā budda an yakūna ḥādithan). This is a foundational argument for the origination of the material world.

Key Terms
أكوان أربعة
حركة
سكون
اجتماع
افتراق
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument is circular because it assumes accidents are originated without proof.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that accidents are proven originated by their change, which will be elaborated later. It is not assumed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason substances must be originated?
A) Because they are composed of atoms.
B) Because they are inseparable from originated accidents.
C) Because they move.
D) Because they are possible in themselves.

Key Takeaway
Substances are proven originated because they are inseparable from accidents that are observed to change and thus have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very body is proven to have a beginning because it is inseparable from changing accidents, what does that say about your need for the One who brought it into existence? Do you live as if your existence is contingent upon Allah every moment, or do you feel self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that whatever depends on something originated must itself be originated. What aspects of your life are you treating as if they are self-sufficient, when in reality they are dependent on Allah's constant creation? Will you realign your heart's reliance today?
?
Accidents like motion and rest are constantly changing in your body. Does this awareness make you more conscious of your mortality and your need to prepare for the meeting with your Lord? What are you actually doing to prepare for that inevitable moment?

beginner
3 min
The Proof of the Temporality of Bodies
Sanusi states the proof of the temporality of bodies using the principle of necessary concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core premise 'whatever is necessarily concomitant with temporal things is itself temporal' and how it proves bodies are created.

Core Idea
If bodies were eternal, they couldn't be inseparable from accidents—so they must be temporal.

Sanusi begins with a powerful logical principle: anything that always accompanies temporal things must itself be temporal. He then applies this to the physical universe, showing that bodies—which are never found without accidents—cannot be eternal. This lesson presents that core argument clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to grasp the concise logic. Then read the translation.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the three steps
The argument has three parts: general principle, application to bodies, conclusion. Find each.
3
Understand the key impossibility
Why is it impossible for the eternal to accompany the temporal? Think about the nature of eternity and change.
Sanusi asserts it is self-evident.
4
Reflect on significance
This argument is foundational in kalam for proving the world's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبرهان على حدوث العالم : هو أن ملازم الحادث حادث، وتفصيله أن تقول (1) في بيان حدوث الأجرام ) : لو كانت الأجرام قديمة لزم ملازمتها للأعراض الحادثة ، ويستحيل أن يلازم القديم الحادث، إذن الأجرام حادثة. قد حاول التشكيك في هذه المقدمة أي في قولنا أن كل ما لازم الحوادث فيجب أن يكون حادثاً، وادعى أنها ليست مبرهنة، وليست مقدمة قوية، وهي في الحقيقة كما ترى قوية ومبرهن عليها، ولكن ابن تيمية لما شكك فيها لم يكن تشكيكه نابعاً من نظر محض، أي لم يكن ناظراً إلى نفس القضية، بل كان ناظرا إلى ما يلزم عنها، من نفي قيام الحوادث بالله تعالى، وهي العقيدة التي يقول بها السادة الأشاعرة بل كل الفرق الإسلامية عدا المجسمة ومنهم ابن تيمية، فإنه لما لاحظ ذلك حاول جهده أن ينقض هذه المقدمة، ولكن كان كل ما أتى به ضعيفاً، وهو في الواقع لم يأت بشيء إلا ادعاءه أن هذه المقدمة لم يأت بها الكتاب والسنة ، ولم يستطع أن يدلل على أنها تناقض العقول مطلقاً، وقد رد عليه أهل السنة ودللوا على أن الكتاب والسنة فيهما أدلة على هذه المقدمة، وعلى تنزيه الله تعالى عن قيام الحوادث به وهو أي ابن تيمية - من القائلين بأن الحوادث تقوم بذات الله تعالى، فتقوم فيه الحركة فإنه عنده ينتقل من محل إلى آخر، وتقوم به إرادة حادثة، وذلك عندما يريد أن يخلق أي مخلوق جديد وتقوم به الأصوات والحروف التي يدعي أنها كلامه تعالى وصفته القائمان بذاته، ويقوم به الغضب الحادث عند ملاحظة الله تعالى للكافرين وأعمالهم، ويقوم به الانتقام الحادث كذلك، ويقوم به الرضا الحادث، وغير ذلك من الحوادث التي تقوم بالله تعالى عند ابن تيمية، وهذا كله مخالف لما نص عليه علماء أهل السنة، ومخالف لعقائد الإسلام. ولم يقم عليه دليل عقلي ولا نقلي واحد. والقول بقيام الحوادث من الأمور التي بدع علماء الإسلام بها ابن تيمية.فاحذر أيها المسلم من هذه الأقوال الفاسدة.(س). (۱) اعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها وهو الأعراض وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما ، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول : لا نسلم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فنبطله نحن بالمشاهدة، إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويحس أن لذاته شيئا زائداً عليها. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام، فنبطله بمشاهدة عدم الانفكاك. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام ، لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أول لها ، إذ ما من حركة إلا وقبلها حركة ،وهكذا، فتكون حادثة بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديم وشخصها حادث فنبطله بأمور عديدة ؛ منها أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شخصه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً لزم أن يكون النوع كذلك فبطل حوادث لا أول لها .(ج).
And the proof of the temporality of the world is: that the necessary concomitant of the temporal is temporal. Its detail is that you say: (1) In the explanation of the temporality of bodies: If bodies were eternal, they would be necessarily concomitant with temporal accidents, and it is impossible for the eternal to be concomitant with the temporal, therefore bodies are temporal. This premise—i.e., our statement that everything that is concomitant with temporal things must be temporal—has been doubted, and [the doubter] claimed that it is not demonstrated and not a strong premise. In truth it is, as you see, strong and demonstrated. But when Ibn Taymiyyah doubted it, his doubt was not based on pure consideration—i.e., he was not looking at the proposition itself—rather he was looking at what follows from it, namely the negation of the occurrence of temporal things in God Almighty. This is the doctrine held by the Ashʿarī masters and indeed all Islamic sects except the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima), among whom is Ibn Taymiyyah. When he noted that, he tried his utmost to refute this premise, but all he brought was weak. In reality, he brought nothing except his claim that this premise is not [found] in the Book and the Sunna, and he was unable to prove that it contradicts the intellects absolutely. The Ahl al-Sunna have responded to him and demonstrated that the Book and the Sunna contain evidence for this premise and for declaring God transcendent of the occurrence of temporal things in Him. And he—i.e., Ibn Taymiyyah—is among those who say that temporal things occur in God's essence. So movement occurs in Him—for according to him He moves from one place to another—and temporal will occurs in Him when He wills to create any new creature, and sounds and letters occur in Him which he claims are His speech and attribute subsisting in His essence, and temporal anger occurs in Him when God observes the disbelievers and their deeds, and temporal vengeance likewise occurs in Him, and temporal pleasure occurs in Him, and other temporal things that occur in God according to Ibn Taymiyyah. All of this contradicts what the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna have stipulated and contradicts the beliefs of Islam. No rational or transmitted proof has been established for it. The claim that temporal things occur [in God] is one of the matters for which the scholars of Islam have declared Ibn Taymiyyah an innovator. So beware, O Muslim, of these corrupt sayings. (s). (1) Know that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on establishing something additional to them—namely accidents—and on establishing the necessary concomitance between them, and on refuting the [notion of] beginningless temporal events. That is because the opponent might say: We do not accept that there is something additional to bodies. We refute that by observation, for no rational person but feels that his essence has something additional to it. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept the necessary concomitance between it and bodies. We refute that by observing that they are never separate. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept that it indicates the temporality of bodies, because it is possible that [bodies] are eternal and that the additional thing is a series of beginningless temporal events. For no movement exists but that a movement preceded it, and so on. Thus [the accidents] would be temporal as individuals but eternal as a species—meaning that the species of movement is eternal and its individuals are temporal. We refute that by several things, among them: that the species has no existence except within its individuals. So if the individual is temporal, it follows that the species is likewise. Thus beginningless temporal events are invalidated. (j).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajrām) and a refutation of objections, particularly those of Ibn Taymiyyah. The author begins by stating the overall proof of the world's temporality: the necessary concomitant of the temporal is itself temporal (mulāzim al-ḥādith ḥādith). He then applies this to bodies: if bodies were eternal, they would necessarily be accompanied by temporal accidents (aʿrāḍ), but it is impossible for the eternal to be accompanied by the temporal, so bodies are temporal. Next, the author addresses a doubt raised against the premise that 'everything concomitant with temporal things must be temporal.' The doubter (Ibn Taymiyyah) claimed it is not demonstrated. The author asserts that it is indeed strong and demonstrated. He explains that Ibn Taymiyyah's objection stems not from a neutral examination of the premise itself but from his concern about its consequence: that it negates the occurrence of temporal things in God. The author then contrasts the Ashʿarī position of God's transcendence with Ibn Taymiyyah's anthropomorphism, listing several temporal attributes that Ibn Taymiyyah attributes to God (movement, will, sounds, anger, etc.) and notes that this contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. He warns the reader against such corrupt sayings. The passage then provides a more detailed logical structuring of the proof. The author says that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on three things: (1) establishing that there is something additional to bodies, namely accidents; (2) establishing the necessary concomitance between bodies and accidents; and (3) refuting the possibility of beginningless temporal events (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā). He then presents a dialectical exchange with an opponent: The opponent may deny that there is anything additional to bodies, but we refute that by observation (every rational person feels something additional to his essence). The opponent then accepts that but denies the necessary concomitance; we refute that by observing that bodies are never devoid of accidents. The opponent then accepts that but argues that this does not prove bodies are temporal because the accidents could be a series of beginningless events—each accident is temporal but the species is eternal (e.g., each movement is preceded by another movement, so the type is eternal). The author refutes this by noting that a species has no existence except in its individuals; if every individual is temporal, the species cannot be eternal. Thus, beginningless temporal events are impossible.

Key Terms
ملازم الحادث حادث
حدوث الأجرام
الأعراض
قديم

Checkpoint
What is the core principle used in the proof?
A) The world is created
B) The necessary concomitant of the temporal is temporal
C) Bodies have accidents

Key Takeaway
The temporality of bodies is proved by the fact that they are inseparable from temporal accidents, and the eternal cannot be inseparable from the temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that everything material around you—your body, your possessions, the earth—is temporal and will end. If you truly believed this, how would it change the way you hold onto your wealth, your health, or your relationships? Are there things you cling to as if they will never perish? What would it take for you to loosen your grip?
?
The proof is rational, but the Quran calls us to reflect on the heavens and earth as signs. Do you look at the world and see its temporality, or do you live as though it were permanent? When you walk outside tomorrow, will you see the trees and mountains as created, finite, and pointing to the Eternal? If not, why do you think your heart is so slow to accept what your mind knows?
?
This argument depends on the principle that 'the necessary concomitant of the temporal is temporal.' But what about your own heart? It is filled with temporal desires and attachments. Is it possible that you have made a 'temporal' thing—a person, a goal, a comfort—an eternal god in your life? How can you use this proof to remind yourself daily that only Allah is eternal, and everything else is passing away?

Refutation of Ibn Taymiyyah's Doubt
Sanusi refutes Ibn Taymiyyah's challenge and explains its root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Ibn Taymiyyah's objection to the premise and why it is unfounded.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyyah's doubt arose from his theological commitments, not logic.

Having presented the proof, Sanusi now addresses a challenge from Ibn Taymiyyah, who claimed the premise 'whatever is concomitant with temporal things is temporal' is not proven. Sanusi reveals that the real motivation behind this doubt is Ibn Taymiyyah's belief that temporal events can occur in God, which would be denied by the premise. This lesson shows how personal beliefs can cloud rational judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read how Sanusi characterizes Ibn Taymiyyah's doubt and his response.
Notice the shift from logical to theological discussion.
2
Identify the motivation
Sanusi says the doubt is not from pure consideration but from concern about the consequence: negating temporal events in God.
3
Understand the opposing views
Contrast Ash'ari position (God transcends temporal events) with Ibn Taymiyyah's anthropomorphism.
List the temporal attributes Sanusi mentions.
4
Reflect on the warning
Sanusi warns the Muslim to beware of these corrupt sayings.

(۱) اعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها وهو الأعراض وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما ، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول : لا نسلم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فنبطله نحن بالمشاهدة، إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويحس أن لذاته شيئا زائداً عليها. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام، فنبطله بمشاهدة عدم الانفكاك. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام ، لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أول لها ، إذ ما من حركة إلا وقبلها حركة ،وهكذا، فتكون حادثة بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديم وشخصها حادث فنبطله بأمور عديدة ؛ منها أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شخصه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً لزم أن يكون النوع كذلك فبطل حوادث لا أول لها .(ج).
And the proof of the temporality of the world is: that the necessary concomitant of the temporal is temporal. Its detail is that you say: (1) In the explanation of the temporality of bodies: If bodies were eternal, they would be necessarily concomitant with temporal accidents, and it is impossible for the eternal to be concomitant with the temporal, therefore bodies are temporal. The claim that temporal things occur [in God] is one of the matters for which the scholars of Islam have declared Ibn Taymiyyah an innovator. So beware, O Muslim, of these corrupt sayings. (s). For no movement exists but that a movement preceded it, and so on. We refute that by several things, among them: that the species has no existence except within its individuals. So if the individual is temporal, it follows that the species is likewise. Thus beginningless temporal events are invalidated. (j).

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajrām) and a refutation of objections, particularly those of Ibn Taymiyyah. The author begins by stating the overall proof of the world's temporality: the necessary concomitant of the temporal is itself temporal (mulāzim al-ḥādith ḥādith). The author then contrasts the Ashʿarī position of God's transcendence with Ibn Taymiyyah's anthropomorphism, listing several temporal attributes that Ibn Taymiyyah attributes to God (movement, will, sounds, anger, etc.) and notes that this contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. He warns the reader against such corrupt sayings. The passage then provides a more detailed logical structuring of the proof. The author says that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on three things: (1) establishing that there is something additional to bodies, namely accidents; (2) establishing the necessary concomitance between bodies and accidents; and (3) refuting the possibility of beginningless temporal events (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā). He then presents a dialectical exchange with an opponent: The opponent may deny that there is anything additional to bodies, but we refute that by observation (every rational person feels something additional to his essence). The opponent then accepts that but denies the necessary concomitance; we refute that by observing that bodies are never devoid of accidents. The opponent then accepts that but argues that this does not prove bodies are temporal because the accidents could be a series of beginningless events—each accident is temporal but the species is eternal (e.g., each movement is preceded by another movement, so the type is eternal). The author refutes this by noting that a species has no existence except in its individuals; if every individual is temporal, the species cannot be eternal. Thus, beginningless temporal events are impossible.

Correction: Sanusi states that Ahl al-Sunna have demonstrated it from Quran and Sunnah.

Detailed Logical Structure of the Proof
Sanusi details the logical steps and dialectical refutation of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three pillars of the proof of temporality of bodies and how to refute the objection of beginningless temporal events.

Core Idea
The proof rests on accidents, their inseparability from bodies, and the impossibility of an eternal series of temporal events.

Sanusi now unpacks the proof into three distinct steps: establishing the existence of accidents, proving their necessary concomitance with bodies, and refuting the possibility that accidents are a beginningless chain. He presents a dialectical exchange with an opponent, showing how each challenge is answered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three requirements and the opponent's objections.
The passage has a clear dialectical format.
2
Identify the three prerequisites
Sanusi lists three things the proof depends on. Find each one.
3
Follow the dialectic
The opponent makes three objections; see how Sanusi refutes each.
First: existence of accidents. Second: necessary concomitance. Third: beginningless accidents.
4
Focus on the final refutation
The key point: a species has no existence apart from its individuals. If all individuals are temporal, the species cannot be eternal.
5
Reflect on completeness
This shows the proof is comprehensive, addressing potential counterarguments.

والبرهان على حدوث العالم : هو أن ملازم الحادث حادث، وتفصيله أن تقول (1) في بيان حدوث الأجرام ) : لو كانت الأجرام قديمة لزم ملازمتها للأعراض الحادثة ، ويستحيل أن يلازم القديم الحادث، إذن الأجرام حادثة. قد حاول التشكيك في هذه المقدمة أي في قولنا أن كل ما لازم الحوادث فيجب أن يكون حادثاً، وادعى أنها ليست مبرهنة، وليست مقدمة قوية، وهي في الحقيقة كما ترى قوية ومبرهن عليها، ولكن ابن تيمية لما شكك فيها لم يكن تشكيكه نابعاً من نظر محض، أي لم يكن ناظراً إلى نفس القضية، بل كان ناظرا إلى ما يلزم عنها، من نفي قيام الحوادث بالله تعالى، وهي العقيدة التي يقول بها السادة الأشاعرة بل كل الفرق الإسلامية عدا المجسمة ومنهم ابن تيمية، فإنه لما لاحظ ذلك حاول جهده أن ينقض هذه المقدمة، ولكن كان كل ما أتى به ضعيفاً، وهو في الواقع لم يأت بشيء إلا ادعاءه أن هذه المقدمة لم يأت بها الكتاب والسنة ، ولم يستطع أن يدلل على أنها تناقض العقول مطلقاً، وقد رد عليه أهل السنة ودللوا على أن الكتاب والسنة فيهما أدلة على هذه المقدمة، وعلى تنزيه الله تعالى عن قيام الحوادث به وهو أي ابن تيمية - من القائلين بأن الحوادث تقوم بذات الله تعالى، فتقوم فيه الحركة فإنه عنده ينتقل من محل إلى آخر، وتقوم به إرادة حادثة، وذلك عندما يريد أن يخلق أي مخلوق جديد وتقوم به الأصوات والحروف التي يدعي أنها كلامه تعالى وصفته القائمان بذاته، ويقوم به الغضب الحادث عند ملاحظة الله تعالى للكافرين وأعمالهم، ويقوم به الانتقام الحادث كذلك، ويقوم به الرضا الحادث، وغير ذلك من الحوادث التي تقوم بالله تعالى عند ابن تيمية، وهذا كله مخالف لما نص عليه علماء أهل السنة، ومخالف لعقائد الإسلام. ولم يقم عليه دليل عقلي ولا نقلي واحد. والقول بقيام الحوادث من الأمور التي بدع علماء الإسلام بها ابن تيمية.فاحذر أيها المسلم من هذه الأقوال الفاسدة.(س). (۱) اعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها وهو الأعراض وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما ، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول : لا نسلم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فنبطله نحن بالمشاهدة، إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويحس أن لذاته شيئا زائداً عليها. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام، فنبطله بمشاهدة عدم الانفكاك. فيقول : سلمنا ذلك ، لكن لا نسلم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام ، لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أول لها ، إذ ما من حركة إلا وقبلها حركة ،وهكذا، فتكون حادثة بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديم وشخصها حادث فنبطله بأمور عديدة ؛ منها أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شخصه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً لزم أن يكون النوع كذلك فبطل حوادث لا أول لها .(ج).
And the proof of the temporality of the world is: that the necessary concomitant of the temporal is temporal. Its detail is that you say: (1) In the explanation of the temporality of bodies: If bodies were eternal, they would be necessarily concomitant with temporal accidents, and it is impossible for the eternal to be concomitant with the temporal, therefore bodies are temporal. This premise—i.e., our statement that everything that is concomitant with temporal things must be temporal—has been doubted, and [the doubter] claimed that it is not demonstrated and not a strong premise. In truth it is, as you see, strong and demonstrated. But when Ibn Taymiyyah doubted it, his doubt was not based on pure consideration—i.e., he was not looking at the proposition itself—rather he was looking at what follows from it, namely the negation of the occurrence of temporal things in God Almighty. This is the doctrine held by the Ashʿarī masters and indeed all Islamic sects except the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima), among whom is Ibn Taymiyyah. When he noted that, he tried his utmost to refute this premise, but all he brought was weak. In reality, he brought nothing except his claim that this premise is not [found] in the Book and the Sunna, and he was unable to prove that it contradicts the intellects absolutely. The Ahl al-Sunna have responded to him and demonstrated that the Book and the Sunna contain evidence for this premise and for declaring God transcendent of the occurrence of temporal things in Him. And he—i.e., Ibn Taymiyyah—is among those who say that temporal things occur in God's essence. So movement occurs in Him—for according to him He moves from one place to another—and temporal will occurs in Him when He wills to create any new creature, and sounds and letters occur in Him which he claims are His speech and attribute subsisting in His essence, and temporal anger occurs in Him when God observes the disbelievers and their deeds, and temporal vengeance likewise occurs in Him, and temporal pleasure occurs in Him, and other temporal things that occur in God according to Ibn Taymiyyah. All of this contradicts what the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna have stipulated and contradicts the beliefs of Islam. No rational or transmitted proof has been established for it. The claim that temporal things occur [in God] is one of the matters for which the scholars of Islam have declared Ibn Taymiyyah an innovator. So beware, O Muslim, of these corrupt sayings. (s). (1) Know that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on establishing something additional to them—namely accidents—and on establishing the necessary concomitance between them, and on refuting the [notion of] beginningless temporal events. That is because the opponent might say: We do not accept that there is something additional to bodies. We refute that by observation, for no rational person but feels that his essence has something additional to it. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept the necessary concomitance between it and bodies. We refute that by observing that they are never separate. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept that it indicates the temporality of bodies, because it is possible that [bodies] are eternal and that the additional thing is a series of beginningless temporal events. For no movement exists but that a movement preceded it, and so on. Thus [the accidents] would be temporal as individuals but eternal as a species—meaning that the species of movement is eternal and its individuals are temporal. We refute that by several things, among them: that the species has no existence except within its individuals. So if the individual is temporal, it follows that the species is likewise. Thus beginningless temporal events are invalidated. (j).

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajrām) and a refutation of objections, particularly those of Ibn Taymiyyah. The author begins by stating the overall proof of the world's temporality: the necessary concomitant of the temporal is itself temporal (mulāzim al-ḥādith ḥādith). He then applies this to bodies: if bodies were eternal, they would necessarily be accompanied by temporal accidents (aʿrāḍ), but it is impossible for the eternal to be accompanied by the temporal, so bodies are temporal. Next, the author addresses a doubt raised against the premise that 'everything concomitant with temporal things must be temporal.' The doubter (Ibn Taymiyyah) claimed it is not demonstrated. The author asserts that it is indeed strong and demonstrated. He explains that Ibn Taymiyyah's objection stems not from a neutral examination of the premise itself but from his concern about its consequence: that it negates the occurrence of temporal things in God. The author then contrasts the Ashʿarī position of God's transcendence with Ibn Taymiyyah's anthropomorphism, listing several temporal attributes that Ibn Taymiyyah attributes to God (movement, will, sounds, anger, etc.) and notes that this contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna. He warns the reader against such corrupt sayings. The passage then provides a more detailed logical structuring of the proof. The author says that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on three things: (1) establishing that there is something additional to bodies, namely accidents; (2) establishing the necessary concomitance between bodies and accidents; and (3) refuting the possibility of beginningless temporal events (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā). He then presents a dialectical exchange with an opponent: The opponent may deny that there is anything additional to bodies, but we refute that by observation (every rational person feels something additional to his essence). The opponent then accepts that but denies the necessary concomitance; we refute that by observing that bodies are never devoid of accidents. The opponent then accepts that but argues that this does not prove bodies are temporal because the accidents could be a series of beginningless events—each accident is temporal but the species is eternal (e.g., each movement is preceded by another movement, so the type is eternal). The author refutes this by noting that a species has no existence except in its individuals; if every individual is temporal, the species cannot be eternal. Thus, beginningless temporal events are impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an infinite series of temporal events (accidents) is possible as a species being eternal.
Correction: Sanusi shows that a species has no existence apart from its individuals, so if each individual is temporal, the species cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute the idea that accidents are beginningless as a type?

B) He argues that a species cannot exist without its individuals, and if all individuals are temporal, the species must be temporal

Key Takeaway
The proof of temporality is robust because it addresses and refutes the possibility of an eternal chain of accidents through the principle that a species depends on its individual instances.

The Proof from Observed Change
Sanusi states the proof from observation and its logical construction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed change of accidents from non-existence to existence demonstrates their temporality, and the logical structure of this proof.

Core Idea
Accidents must be temporal because their change is observed and the eternal cannot change.

Sanusi begins by stating the most immediate proof for the temporality of accidents: direct observation of their change. He then explains the logical construction (tarkīb) of this proof, showing that it is an exceptive syllogism. A subtle note clarifies that this proof assumes accidents endure for two moments. This lesson unpacks that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the main proof statement
Read the Arabic passage from 'ودليل حدوث الأعراض' up to 'فما صدق على أحدهما صدق على الآخر'. Notice that the proof is based on observed change.
The observation is of change from non-existence to existence and vice versa.
2
Understand the note about duration
The passage notes that this proof holds if we say the accident endures for two moments. Consider why this matters: if accidents do not endure, their very occurrence is change.
3
Study the logical construction
Read from 'وتركيب البرهان' to the end of the logical structure. This is an exceptive syllogism: if accidents were eternal, they would not change (impossible for eternal to change). But we observe they do change, so they are not eternal.
4
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Ask yourself: is observation sufficient to prove temporality for all accidents? The next lessons will address objections and extend the proof by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدوث الأعراض مشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى وجود ومن وجود إلى (۲) عدم. وما لم نشاهد فيه التغير فهو قابل له ) ، ويقاس على الأول لتساويهما في أصل الجرمية، فما صدق على أحدهما صدق على الآخر. ملاحظة : هذا البرهان يتم إذا قلنا أن العرض يبقى زمانين ، وأما إذا قلنا إن العرض لا يبقى زمانين فكلها متغيرة بالحصول دون القبول. وتركيب البرهان أن تقول : لو كانت الأعراض قديمة ( هذه القضية ملزومةٌ)، لما تغيرت (وهذه لازم لها) لاستحالة تغير القديم وهذا بيان الملازمة، لكن نفي تغير الأعراض محال (وهي الاستثنائية) ، (بيانها) المشاهدة. (۱) دليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله لغيره، وإبطال كمونه وإبطال أن القديم ينعدم، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يمنع أنها تتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه فالحركة بعد السكون مثلاً لم تكن معدومة ثم وجدت بل كانت موجودة قبل ذلك، فنقول له : هل كانت قائمة حينئذ بنفسها أو انتقلت من محلها لمحل آخر أو كمنت في محلها، فإن كان الأول لزم قيام العرض بنفسه وهو باطل، وإن كان الثاني فكذلك ، لأنه يلزم قيام العرض بنفسه في لحظة الانتقال، وإن كان الثالث لزم اجتماع الضدين وهو باطل. فيقول سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم أنه يدل على حدوثها لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وتتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه، فنبطله بأن القديم لا ينعدم (ج). (٢) يمكن أن يقال : إن الأعراض التي شاهدناها في العالم منها ما شاهدنا حدوثه، كتحرك الأشجار وغيرها، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائما متحرك ولم نره ساكناً، مثل القمر والكواكب، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائماً ساكن ولم نر أنه يتحرك مثل الجبال. فالقسم الأول حادث بالمشاهدة، والقسمان الآخران كل منهما حادث بالقياس على الأول، لأن الجامع بينهما هو الجسمية، أي إن كل ذلك أجسام وأجرام، والأجرام لها نفس الأحكام، فما جاز على بعضها يجب جوازه على الآخر، فلما جازت الحركة والسكون على بعضها علمنا بالقياس القطعي أنها جائزة على ما لم نشاهد تغيره من القسمين الآخرين، وإذا ثبت جوازها على القسم الغائب عن حواسنا فيلزمه الحدوث. فافهم (س)
And the proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. And whatever we do not observe change in is susceptible to it, and it is measured by analogy to the first because they are equal in the essence of corporeality (jirmiyya). So what is true of one is true of the other. Note: This proof holds if we say that the accident endures for two moments. But if we say that the accident does not endure for two moments, then all [accidents] are changing by [their very] occurrence, not by [their] receptivity. And the construction of the proof is that you say: If accidents were eternal (this proposition is the antecedent) then they would not change (this is its consequent) because change of the eternal is impossible. This is the explanation of the implication. But the denial of change of accidents is impossible (this is the exceptive premise), and its proof is observation. (1) The proof of the temporality of accidents depends on refuting that an accident subsists by itself, refuting its transfer to another [substrate], refuting its latency (kumūn), and refuting that the eternal can cease to exist. That is because the opponent might deny that [accidents] change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. For example, movement after rest was not non-existent and then came into existence; rather it was existent before that. So we say to him: Was it then subsisting by itself? Or did it transfer from its place to another place? Or did it remain latent (mukmin) in its place? If the first, then the accident subsisting by itself would be necessary, which is false. If the second, likewise, because it would necessitate the accident subsisting by itself at the moment of transfer. If the third, then the conjunction of opposites would be necessary, which is false. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept that it indicates its temporality, because it is possible that it is eternal and changes from non-existence to existence and vice versa. We refute that by [the principle] that the eternal does not cease to exist. (j). (2) It can be said: The accidents that we have observed in the world include those whose temporality we have observed, like the movement of trees and others. And among them are those which we observe to be always moving and we have not seen them at rest, like the moon and planets. And among them are those which we observe to be always at rest and we have not seen them move, like mountains. The first category is temporal by observation. The other two categories are each temporal by analogy to the first, because the common ground (al-jāmiʿ) between them is corporeality (al-jismiyya), i.e., all of them are bodies and frames (ajsām wa ajrām), and bodies have the same judgments. So what is possible for some must be possible for the others. Thus, when movement and rest are possible for some, we know by definitive analogy that they are possible for those whose change we have not observed from the other two categories. And when their possibility is established for the category absent from our senses, temporality follows from it. So understand. (s).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of accidents (ḥudūth al-aʿrāḍ). The author begins by stating that the proof is observation (mushāhada) of their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. He then extends this to all accidents: even those we do not observe changing are susceptible to change, and we judge them by analogy (qiyās) to those we have observed because they share the essence of corporeality (aṣl al-jirmiyya). The author then notes a subtle point: this proof holds if we say that an accident endures for two moments (i.e., has duration). If we adopt the view that accidents do not endure for two moments, then all accidents are changing by their very occurrence, not by undergoing change. Next, he presents the logical structure (tarkīb al-burhān): If accidents were eternal, they would not change (because change of the eternal is impossible). But observation shows that they do change, so they cannot be eternal. The proof is an exceptive syllogism (istithnāʾiyya). The author then dives deeper into the proof by addressing potential objections. He says the proof depends on refuting four possibilities that an opponent might propose to avoid admitting that accidents change from non-existence to existence: (1) that the accident subsists by itself (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bi-nafsih), (2) that it transfers from one substrate to another (intiqāluhu li-ghayrihi), (3) that it is latent (kumūn) in its substrate, and (4) that the eternal can cease to exist (an al-qadīm yanʿadim). The opponent might claim that movement after rest was not created anew but was already existing in some form. The author counters by asking whether it was subsisting by itself (which is false because accidents require a substrate), whether it transferred (which would entail subsistence by itself at the moment of transfer), or whether it was latent (which would entail the conjunction of opposites, since the substrate would simultaneously have both rest and movement). The opponent then concedes this but argues that accidents could be eternal yet change (i.e., pass from non-existence to existence repeatedly). The author refutes this by the principle that the eternal cannot cease to exist (al-qadīm lā yanʿadim). Since accidents undergo non-existence, they cannot be eternal. Finally, the author discusses a further extension: observed accidents include those we see change (e.g., trees moving), those we always see moving (e.g., planets), and those we always see at rest (e.g., mountains). The first are temporal by direct observation. The other two are temporal by analogy because they share the common property of being bodies. Since movement and rest are possible for some bodies, they must be possible for all bodies, and once possibility is established, temporality follows (because change is possible only for temporal things). The author concludes by urging the reader to understand.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the logical form of the proof for the temporality of accidents?
A) A categorical syllogism.
B) An exceptive syllogism (istithnāʾiyya).
C) A disjunctive syllogism.
D) An analogical argument.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of accidents is proven by the observation of their change, formalized as an exceptive syllogism: the eternal cannot change, but accidents are observed to change, so they are not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the eternal cannot change, and that change is a sign of temporality. Look at your own life: what is one habit, belief, or identity you have held onto as if it were eternal, even though you know it has changed before? Are you ready to let it go, or will you continue to cling to what is passing?
?
The proof begins with observation of change. When you observe your own emotions—anger, envy, fear—do you treat them as permanent parts of yourself or as accidents that come and go? How would your spiritual discipline change if you consistently saw these states as temporal accidents rather than fixed traits?
?
If all accidents are temporal and you are constantly changing, then your relationship with the Eternal must be built on something that does not change. What in your life right now is truly anchored in the Eternal, and what is merely built on temporary feelings or circumstances? Name one concrete action you will take today to shift your foundation.
