Refuting Objections to the Temporality of Accidents
Sanusi refutes four objections to the temporality of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes four objections that could be raised against the proof of the temporality of accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be eternal because they cannot subsist by themselves, transfer, or remain latent, and the eternal cannot cease to exist.

After presenting the proof from observation, Sanusi anticipates objections. An opponent might claim that what appears as change is actually an accident that has always existed in some other form. Sanusi systematically refutes four possibilities: that an accident subsists by itself, transfers to another substrate, lies latent, or that an eternal being can cease to exist. This lesson walks through each refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on objections
Read from '(۱) دليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال...' to the end of the first section. Note the four things that must be refuted.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says an accident like movement after rest was not created anew; it was already existing. Sanusi then asks three questions to force the opponent into contradiction.
3
Trace the three refutations
Subsistence by itself, transfer, and latency. Each is shown to be impossible. Then the opponent concedes but argues that accidents could be eternal and cycle from non-existence to existence.
4
Learn the principle 'the eternal does not cease to exist'
Sanusi refutes the fourth objection by stating that the eternal cannot cease to exist. Since accidents undergo non-existence, they cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدوث الأعراض مشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى وجود ومن وجود إلى (۲) عدم. وما لم نشاهد فيه التغير فهو قابل له ) ، ويقاس على الأول لتساويهما في أصل الجرمية، فما صدق على أحدهما صدق على الآخر. ملاحظة : هذا البرهان يتم إذا قلنا أن العرض يبقى زمانين ، وأما إذا قلنا إن العرض لا يبقى زمانين فكلها متغيرة بالحصول دون القبول. وتركيب البرهان أن تقول : لو كانت الأعراض قديمة ( هذه القضية ملزومةٌ)، لما تغيرت (وهذه لازم لها) لاستحالة تغير القديم وهذا بيان الملازمة، لكن نفي تغير الأعراض محال (وهي الاستثنائية) ، (بيانها) المشاهدة. (۱) دليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله لغيره، وإبطال كمونه وإبطال أن القديم ينعدم، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يمنع أنها تتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه فالحركة بعد السكون مثلاً لم تكن معدومة ثم وجدت بل كانت موجودة قبل ذلك، فنقول له : هل كانت قائمة حينئذ بنفسها أو انتقلت من محلها لمحل آخر أو كمنت في محلها، فإن كان الأول لزم قيام العرض بنفسه وهو باطل، وإن كان الثاني فكذلك ، لأنه يلزم قيام العرض بنفسه في لحظة الانتقال، وإن كان الثالث لزم اجتماع الضدين وهو باطل. فيقول سلمنا ذلك لكن لا نسلم أنه يدل على حدوثها لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة وتتغير من عدم إلى وجود وعكسه، فنبطله بأن القديم لا ينعدم (ج). (٢) يمكن أن يقال : إن الأعراض التي شاهدناها في العالم منها ما شاهدنا حدوثه، كتحرك الأشجار وغيرها، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائما متحرك ولم نره ساكناً، مثل القمر والكواكب، ومنها ما شاهدنا أنه دائماً ساكن ولم نر أنه يتحرك مثل الجبال. فالقسم الأول حادث بالمشاهدة، والقسمان الآخران كل منهما حادث بالقياس على الأول، لأن الجامع بينهما هو الجسمية، أي إن كل ذلك أجسام وأجرام، والأجرام لها نفس الأحكام، فما جاز على بعضها يجب جوازه على الآخر، فلما جازت الحركة والسكون على بعضها علمنا بالقياس القطعي أنها جائزة على ما لم نشاهد تغيره من القسمين الآخرين، وإذا ثبت جوازها على القسم الغائب عن حواسنا فيلزمه الحدوث. فافهم (س)
And the proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. And whatever we do not observe change in is susceptible to it, and it is measured by analogy to the first because they are equal in the essence of corporeality (jirmiyya). So what is true of one is true of the other. Note: This proof holds if we say that the accident endures for two moments. But if we say that the accident does not endure for two moments, then all [accidents] are changing by [their very] occurrence, not by [their] receptivity. And the construction of the proof is that you say: If accidents were eternal (this proposition is the antecedent) then they would not change (this is its consequent) because change of the eternal is impossible. This is the explanation of the implication. But the denial of change of accidents is impossible (this is the exceptive premise), and its proof is observation. (1) The proof of the temporality of accidents depends on refuting that an accident subsists by itself, refuting its transfer to another [substrate], refuting its latency (kumūn), and refuting that the eternal can cease to exist. That is because the opponent might deny that [accidents] change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. For example, movement after rest was not non-existent and then came into existence; rather it was existent before that. So we say to him: Was it then subsisting by itself? Or did it transfer from its place to another place? Or did it remain latent (mukmin) in its place? If the first, then the accident subsisting by itself would be necessary, which is false. If the second, likewise, because it would necessitate the accident subsisting by itself at the moment of transfer. If the third, then the conjunction of opposites would be necessary, which is false. He says: We accept that, but we do not accept that it indicates its temporality, because it is possible that it is eternal and changes from non-existence to existence and vice versa. We refute that by [the principle] that the eternal does not cease to exist. (j). (2) It can be said: The accidents that we have observed in the world include those whose temporality we have observed, like the movement of trees and others. And among them are those which we observe to be always moving and we have not seen them at rest, like the moon and planets. And among them are those which we observe to be always at rest and we have not seen them move, like mountains. The first category is temporal by observation. The other two categories are each temporal by analogy to the first, because the common ground (al-jāmiʿ) between them is corporeality (al-jismiyya), i.e., all of them are bodies and frames (ajsām wa ajrām), and bodies have the same judgments. So what is possible for some must be possible for the others. Thus, when movement and rest are possible for some, we know by definitive analogy that they are possible for those whose change we have not observed from the other two categories. And when their possibility is established for the category absent from our senses, temporality follows from it. So understand. (s).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of accidents (ḥudūth al-aʿrāḍ). The author begins by stating that the proof is observation (mushāhada) of their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. He then extends this to all accidents: even those we do not observe changing are susceptible to change, and we judge them by analogy (qiyās) to those we have observed because they share the essence of corporeality (aṣl al-jirmiyya). The author then notes a subtle point: this proof holds if we say that an accident endures for two moments (i.e., has duration). If we adopt the view that accidents do not endure for two moments, then all accidents are changing by their very occurrence, not by undergoing change. Next, he presents the logical structure (tarkīb al-burhān): If accidents were eternal, they would not change (because change of the eternal is impossible). But observation shows that they do change, so they cannot be eternal. The proof is an exceptive syllogism (istithnāʾiyya). The author then dives deeper into the proof by addressing potential objections. He says the proof depends on refuting four possibilities that an opponent might propose to avoid admitting that accidents change from non-existence to existence: (1) that the accident subsists by itself (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bi-nafsih), (2) that it transfers from one substrate to another (intiqāluhu li-ghayrihi), (3) that it is latent (kumūn) in its substrate, and (4) that the eternal can cease to exist (an al-qadīm yanʿadim). The opponent might claim that movement after rest was not created anew but was already existing in some form. The author counters by asking whether it was subsisting by itself (which is false because accidents require a substrate), whether it transferred (which would entail subsistence by itself at the moment of transfer), or whether it was latent (which would entail the conjunction of opposites, since the substrate would simultaneously have both rest and movement). The opponent then concedes this but argues that accidents could be eternal yet change (i.e., pass from non-existence to existence repeatedly). The author refutes this by the principle that the eternal cannot cease to exist (al-qadīm lā yanʿadim). Since accidents undergo non-existence, they cannot be eternal. Finally, the author discusses a further extension: observed accidents include those we see change (e.g., trees moving), those we always see moving (e.g., planets), and those we always see at rest (e.g., mountains). The first are temporal by direct observation. The other two are temporal by analogy because they share the common property of being bodies. Since movement and rest are possible for some bodies, they must be possible for all bodies, and once possibility is established, temporality follows (because change is possible only for temporal things). The author concludes by urging the reader to understand.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بنفسه
انتقال العرض
كمون العرض
قديم لا ينعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an accident can be eternal and yet change from non-existence to existence (e.g., a movement that was always there but appeared at certain times).
Correction: Sanusi refutes this by stating the principle that the eternal does not cease to exist (al-qadīm lā yanʿadim). Since accidents do cease to exist, they cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi use to refute the opponent's claim that an accident could be eternal and change from non-existence to existence?
A) The impossibility of latency.
B) The principle that the eternal does not cease to exist.
C) The impossibility of transfer.
D) Direct observation of change.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of accidents is further strengthened by refuting four objections: accidents cannot subsist alone, transfer, lie latent, and the eternal cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent in this passage clings to a possibility (that accidents are eternal) without examining its logical implications. Is there an area in your life where you hold onto a convenient belief because you haven't fully traced its consequences? What would happen if you applied Sanusi's rigorous questioning to that belief?
?
If the eternal cannot cease to exist, and you are a compound of accidents that are constantly changing and passing away, then you are clearly not eternal. Does this proof settle in your heart that you are a temporal creation destined for accountability before Allah? Or do you still act as if you have an eternity to prepare?
?
The opponent concedes the first three absurdities but still resists the conclusion. Are there truths about Allah's attributes that you intellectually accept but resist applying to your life? What specific aspect of Allah's eternity (al-qadīm) do you need to internalize today so that it changes how you live this fleeting moment?

The Analogical Proof for Unobserved Accidents
Sanusi classifies observed accidents and proves temporality by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses analogy (qiyās) to prove that all accidents are temporal, including those not directly observed to change.

Core Idea
Accidents not seen changing are still temporal by analogy, because they share corporeality with observed changing accidents.

Having established the proof for observed accidents, Sanusi extends it to all accidents. He classifies observed accidents into three categories: those we see change, those always moving, and those always at rest. The first is temporal by observation, and the other two are temporal by analogy because they are all bodies, and bodies have the same judgments. This lesson explains that crucial analogical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on analogy
Read from '(۲) يمكن أن يقال' to the end. Notice the three categories of accidents: changing, always moving, always at rest.
2
Identify the analogical reasoning
The common ground (jāmiʿ) is corporeality (jismiyya). Since movement and rest are possible for some bodies, they must be possible for all bodies. Possibility of change entails temporality.
3
Understand why possibility implies temporality
In kalam, if something is possible (jāʾiz) for a body, it can occur, and because bodies are temporal, any possibility of change means the accident is temporal. This completes the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusiveness
Ask yourself: does this analogical argument prove that every accident is temporal? Consider why the author says 'فافهم' (so understand) at the end, emphasizing its importance.

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the temporality of accidents (ḥudūth al-aʿrāḍ). The author begins by stating that the proof is observation (mushāhada) of their change from non-existence to existence and vice versa. He then extends this to all accidents: even those we do not observe changing are susceptible to change, and we judge them by analogy (qiyās) to those we have observed because they share the essence of corporeality (aṣl al-jirmiyya). But observation shows that they do change, so they cannot be eternal. The proof is an exceptive syllogism (istithnāʾiyya). The author then dives deeper into the proof by addressing potential objections. The opponent then concedes this but argues that accidents could be eternal yet change (i.e., pass from non-existence to existence repeatedly). The author refutes this by the principle that the eternal cannot cease to exist (al-qadīm lā yanʿadim). Since accidents undergo non-existence, they cannot be eternal. Finally, the author discusses a further extension: observed accidents include those we see change (e.g., trees moving), those we always see moving (e.g., planets), and those we always see at rest (e.g., mountains). The first are temporal by direct observation. The other two are temporal by analogy because they share the common property of being bodies. Since movement and rest are possible for some bodies, they must be possible for all bodies, and once possibility is established, temporality follows (because change is possible only for temporal things). The author concludes by urging the reader to understand.

According to Sanusi, why are accidents that are always at rest (like mountains) still temporal?

?

Commit today to study these proofs not as academic facts but as foundations of your reliance on Allah.
?

?
If the possibility of change is enough to prove an accident is temporal, what does that say about your own attachment to worldly things? Are you living as if these things are permanent, or are you constantly aware that they can change at any moment? Ask Allah to grant you the certainty that only He remains.

Proof of God's Pre-Eternity and the Necessary-Contingent Distinction
Sanusi proves God's pre-eternity and defines necessary vs. contingent existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that God must be pre-eternal and the distinction between necessary and contingent existence.

Core Idea
God cannot be contingent because contingency leads to impossible circularity or infinite regress.

Sanusi presents a logical proof that God must be pre-eternal. He argues that if God were contingent, He would require a cause, and that cause would require another, leading to absurdities. He then clarifies the difference between necessary and contingent existence. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation to understand the argument's structure.
Notice that the proof is presented as a conditional argument: if not pre-eternal, then contingent, then cause, then absurdity.
2
Understand the classification of existents
The argument begins by dividing all existents into pre-eternal (qadim) and contingent (hadith). There is no third category. Ask: why is this division exhaustive?
A contingent thing is one that could have not existed; a pre-eternal thing has no beginning.
3
See why circularity and infinite regress are impossible
If God were contingent, He would need a cause. If that cause were also contingent, we would have either circularity (each is cause and effect of the other) or infinite regress (an endless chain). Sanusi explains why both are contradictory.
Circularity leads to a thing being both prior and posterior to itself; infinite regress leads to an infinite series that is also finite (since each member enters existence).
4
Grasp the necessary-contingent distinction
In the final note, Sanusi defines necessary existence as that which is not preceded by non-existence and cannot cease, and contingent existence as that which is preceded by non-existence and can cease.
This distinction is foundational for understanding God's nature in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب القدم: الله أنه لو لم يكن قديماً لصار حادثاً جائز الوجود، لأن الموجودات تنحصر في القديم والحادث فلا واسطة بينهما ، وإذا كان حادثاً فلا بد له من محدث أيضاً لأنه لو فرض أنه مثله ثم كذلك ، لزم الدور أو التسلسل، وهما محالان باطلان لأنه يلزم في الدور التقديم والتأخير في وقت واحد لكل منهما فإن كلا منهما فاعل للآخر ومفعول له، وكونه فاعلاً يلزم أن يكون متقدماً على مفعوله، وكونه مفعولاً له يلزم أن يكون متأخراً عنه، وكون الشيء الواحد متقدماً متأخراً في وقت واحد جمع بين النقيضين ويلزم في التسلسل الفراغ وعدم النهاية لأن فرض التسلسل ترتيب أمور غير متناهية ودخولها في الوجود يقتضي أنها متناهية، لأن كل ما دخل في الوجود فهو متناه، فيلزم على القول بالتسلسل جمع بين النقيضين ، والجمع بين النقيضين محال. فإذا بطل الدور والتسلسل بطل حدوث الإله ، وإذا بطل الحدوث وجب القدم وهو المطلوب. ملاحظة : الفرق بين الوجود الجائز والوجود الواجب، أن الوجود الجائز هو الذي سبقه العدم ويمكن لحوق العدم له، وأما الوجود الواجب فهو الذي لا يسبقه العدم ولا يمكن لحوق العدم له.
Proof of the necessity of pre-eternity: God—if He were not pre-eternal, He would become contingent and possible of existence because existing things are confined to the pre-eternal and the contingent, and there is no intermediary between them. And if He were contingent, then He would necessarily have a cause, because if it is supposed that He is like that and so on, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, and both are impossible and false. For in circularity, there is priority and posteriority at one time for each of them, since each is an agent of the other and an effect of it. Being an agent necessitates being prior to its effect, and being an effect necessitates being posterior to it. For a single thing to be both prior and posterior at one time is a conjunction of contradictories. And in infinite regress, there is vacuousness and no end, because supposing infinite regress is an arrangement of infinite things, and their entering into existence entails that they are finite, because everything that enters into existence is finite. Thus, by saying infinite regress, a conjunction of contradictories follows, and the conjunction of contradictories is impossible. So if circularity and infinite regress are invalid, then the contingency of God is invalid, and if contingency is invalid, then pre-eternity is necessary, and that is what is sought. Note: The difference between contingent existence and necessary existence is that contingent existence is that which is preceded by non-existence and can be followed by non-existence, while necessary existence is that which is not preceded by non-existence and cannot be followed by non-existence.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the necessity of God's pre-eternity (qidam) and clarifies the distinction between necessary (wajib) and contingent (ja'iz) existence. The proof begins by noting that all existents are either pre-eternal (qadim) or contingent (hadith); there is no third category. If God were not pre-eternal, He would be contingent, and therefore would require a cause (muhdith). If that cause were similar to Him (i.e., also contingent), then either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would result. Both are impossible: circularity requires a thing to be both prior and posterior to itself, which is a contradiction; infinite regress implies an infinite chain of things entering existence, yet everything that enters existence is finite, so the series would be both infinite and finite—another contradiction. Therefore, God cannot be contingent, so He must be pre-eternal. The note explains the difference: contingent existence is preceded by non-existence and can cease to exist, while necessary existence has no beginning in non-existence and cannot cease to exist.

حدوث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'pre-eternal' merely means 'very old,' but here it means having no beginning in time and being uncaused.
Correction: Pre-eternal (qadim) in this context means that the being has no prior non-existence and is not caused; it is the opposite of contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of supposing that God is contingent?
A) He would have a cause, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
B) He would be timeless.
C) He would be composed of parts.
D) He would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity is proven by showing that contingency leads to circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible, so God must be necessary and uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence is contingent—it began and will end. The proof shows that every contingent being must be caused by something else. Does this truth make you feel anxious about your dependence, or does it drive you to anchor yourself in the Necessary Being? What would change in your daily life if you truly felt your absolute need for God every moment?
?
The argument rules out an infinite chain of causes, meaning you are directly connected to the Creator without intermediaries. How does this affect your sense of personal accountability and relationship with God? Are you living as if there is a 'middleman' between you and your Creator?
?
Circularity would mean that God depends on His creation, but the proof shows that leads to a logical absurdity. Do you ever act as if God needs your worship or obedience, as if you are doing Him a favor? How can you realign your heart to see that your entire existence is a gift from the Self-Sufficient One?

Proof of God's Necessary Pre-Eternity
Sanusi proves God's necessary pre-eternity by refuting circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress leads to the conclusion that God is necessarily pre-eternal.

Core Idea
Neither circularity nor infinite regress can explain originated existence, so God must be pre-eternal.

Sanusi presents a classic kalam proof for God's pre-eternity. He argues that if God were originated, He would require an originator, leading to an impossible chain. Understanding this proof is key to grasping the Islamic theological foundation of God's eternal nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Notice how Sanusi structures the proof as a conditional chain.
The passage uses technical terms like qadim and hadith.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
Locate where Sanusi discusses circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). Understand why each is considered impossible by analyzing the contradictions he points out.
Circularity involves a thing being both prior and posterior; infinite regress involves a contradiction between an infinite series and finite members.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow how Sanusi moves from the impossibility of circularity and regress to the falsity of God's origination, and then to the necessity of pre-eternity.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite, derive an impossibility, therefore the opposite is false.
4
Reflect on foundational importance
Consider why this proof is a cornerstone of kalam theology. It establishes a necessary attribute of God using pure reason, independent of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبرهان وجوب القدم أنه لو لم يكن قديماً لصار حادثاً جائز الوجود، لأن الموجودات تنحصر في القديم والحادث فلا واسطة بينهما ، وإذا كان حادثاً فلا بد له من محدث أيضاً لأنه لو فرض أنه مثله ثم كذلك ، لزم الدور أو التسلسل، وهما محالان باطلان لأنه يلزم في الدور التقديم والتأخير في وقت واحد لكل منهما فإن كلا منهما فاعل للآخر ومفعول له، وكونه فاعلاً يلزم أن يكون متقدماً على مفعوله، وكونه مفعولاً له يلزم أن يكون متأخراً عنه، وكون الشيء الواحد متقدماً متأخراً في وقت واحد جمع بين النقيضين ويلزم في التسلسل الفراغ وعدم النهاية لأن فرض التسلسل ترتيب أمور غير متناهية ودخولها في الوجود يقتضي أنها متناهية، لأن كل ما دخل في الوجود فهو متناه، فيلزم على القول بالتسلسل جمع بين النقيضين ، والجمع بين النقيضين محال. فإذا بطل الدور والتسلسل بطل حدوث الإله ، وإذا بطل الحدوث وجب القدم وهو المطلوب.
The proof of the necessity of pre-eternity: that if He were not pre-eternal, He would become originated (ḥādith), possible of existence (jā'iz al-wujūd), because existents are restricted to the pre-eternal and the originated, with no intermediate between them. And if He were originated, then He must also have an originator (muḥdith), because if it were assumed that He is like it and so on, circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, and both are impossible (muḥāl) and false. For in circularity there follows priority and posteriority at one time for each of them, since each of them is an agent (fā'il) of the other and an object (maf'ūl) of it; being an agent necessitates priority over its effect, and being an object necessitates posteriority to it; and a single thing being both prior and posterior at one time is a combination of contradictories. And in infinite regress there follows void (farāgh) and lack of end, because the assumption of infinite regress is an arrangement of infinite things, and their entering into existence necessitates that they are finite, because everything that enters into existence is finite; thus asserting infinite regress leads to a combination of contradictories, and combining contradictories is impossible. So if circularity and infinite regress are false, then the origination of God is false; and if origination is false, then pre-eternity (qidam) is necessary, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of the necessary pre-eternity (wujūb al-qidam) of God. Sanusi begins by stating the conditional: if God were not pre-eternal, He would be originated and thus possible in existence. He justifies this by asserting that all existents are either pre-eternal or originated, with no category in between. An originated being requires an originator; if that originator were also originated and required another, and so on, the chain would lead either to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). Sanusi then explains why both are impossible. In circularity, each thing is both agent and object of the other, requiring simultaneous priority and posteriority, which is a contradiction. In infinite regress, an infinite series of originated things entering existence would require each to be finite, yet the series is supposedly infinite, also a contradiction. Since both are impossible, the assumption that God is originated is false, therefore He is necessarily pre-eternal. The proof follows the classical kalam method of negating the opposite to establish the positive attribute.

Key Terms
القديم (al-qadīm)
الحادث (al-ḥādith)
الوجود الجائز (al-wujūd al-jā'iz)
الدور (al-dawr)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الجمع بين النقيضين (al-jam' bayn al-naqīḍayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on an assumption that all existents are either pre-eternal or originated, which the opponent might not accept.
Correction: Sanusi presents this division as a basic logical division that the opponent accepts; it is not a hidden assumption but a starting point of the argument.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that an infinite regress of originators is impossible?
A) Because it would lead to the existence of self-caused beings.
B) Because it would require a chain of causes that is both finite and infinite, a contradiction.
C) Because everything that exists must have a beginning.
D) Because the opponent denies the possibility of infinite chains.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that God cannot be originated because that would lead to impossible chains, so He must be pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not pre-eternal, He would be dependent on something else. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment you depend on the Pre-Eternal? Name one specific habit and decide today how you will remind yourself of your dependence on Allah.
?
Sanusi proves that circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Is there any area of your life where your purpose has become circular—living for yourself, as if you are your own end? How can you break that cycle and realign your purpose with the Pre-Eternal God?
?
The proof shows that only God is truly pre-eternal. Have you given any created thing—such as wealth, status, or relationships—an eternal status in your heart, acting as if it will never fade? What step can you take this week to repent and affirm that only Allah is al-Qadīm?

Self-Subsistence Defined and Refutation of Ibn Taymiyya
Sanusi defines self-subsistence and refutes Ibn Taymiyya's denial of the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of self-subsistence and why Ibn Taymiyya's claim of an eternal chain of events is refuted by the hadith.

Core Idea
God's self-subsistence means He needs no place and no creator.

Sanusi begins by defining the key term and then immediately defends it against a deviant interpretation. The definition has two clear parts, and the refutation uses a prophetic hadith to block any suggestion of infinite regress in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening sentences where Sanusi defines self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs) as independence from both a substratum and a specifier.
2
Identify the two components
Notice that self-subsistence is composed of two parts: independence from substratum and independence from specifier.
This will be proved separately later.
3
Read the hadith and refutation
Sanusi cites the hadith 'God was and nothing was before Him' and refutes Ibn Taymiyya's denial of its implication that every creature has a beginning.
Ibn Taymiyya argued for an infinite regress of events.
4
Understand the argument against infinite regress
The hadith and reason both show that every creature has a beginning, so an eternal chain of creatures is impossible.
This establishes the refutation of Ibn Taymiyya's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب قيام الله بنفسه: والقيام بالنفس معناه الغنى عن المحل والمخصص بالذات، فالقيام بالنفس هو الاستغناء بالذات عن المحل والمخصص، فالقيام بالنفس إذن مركب من جزأين : وهذا الحديث خالف ابن تيمية رواياته العديدة الدالة على إثبات أن الله تعالى كان ولم يكن شيء غيره، وادعى أنه لا توجد دلالة على ذلك من الحديث، ورد جميع الألفاظ الواردة إلا كان الله ولم يكن شيء قبله"، بحجج واهية، وذلك تحريفا منه للحديث ليمكنه القول بالقدم النوعي للعالم ، وبناء مذهبه في التجسيم. وقد بينا تفصيل ذلك في تعليقنا على رد الإخميمي عليه وفي كتابنا "الكاشف الصغير عن عقائد ابن تيمية". وروى الإمام أحمد في مسنده عن المحرر بن أبي هريرة عن أبيه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : "لا يزال الناس يسألون حتى يقولوا كان الله قبل كل شيء فما كان قبله". والمراد من ذلك أن السؤال عن بداية كل مخلوق سؤال صحيح، وله جواب لأنه يوجد لكل المخلوقات بداية، وأما السؤال عن بداية لوجود الله تعالى فهو سؤال باطل لأنه لا توجد له جل شأنه بداية. بل هو ثابت قبل كل شيء، وتأمل في هذه الرواية التي تنص على إبطال التسلسل في القدم وتدل عليه دلالة أكيدة، وقد قال ابن تيمية كما ذكرنا بعدم انقطاع سلسلة الحوادث في القدم، مخالفا في قوله ذلك هذه الروايات والآيات الكثيرة التي تدل على أن الله تعالى أوجد الخلق كلهم وجعل لهم بداية، وعليه فيستحيل أن توجد سلسلة من المخلوقات لا بداية لها. وقد بنى ابن تيمية أغلب مذهبه على هذه القاعدة الباطلة. الاستغناء عن المحل والاستغناء عن المخصص. فأما برهان الجزء الأول فلأنه تعالى لو احتاج إلى محل أي ذات يحل فيها كما تحل الصفة في الموصوف، لكان صفة، والصفة معنى من المعاني، فلا يحتاج إلى محل إلا الصفات، والذات لا تحتاج إلى ذات تقوم بها لأن الذات لو قبلت أن تقوم بذات أخرى لزم أن الذات الأخرى تقوم بذات فيلزم التسلسل، لأن القبول وصف نفسي لكل ذات والصفة لا تتصف بصفات المعاني ولا المعنوية لأن الضفة لو قبلت أن تقوم بها صفة لكانت صفتها كذلك، فيلزم التسلسل ودخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود. ملاحظة : مفهوم المعاني والمعنوية أن الصفات تتصف بالصفات النفسية والسلبية بأن يقال علم الله موجود مثلاً فهذه صفة نفسية وكذا غير العلم من صفات المعاني فوجود كل صفة منها نفسي لها ويقال في وصف الصفات بالسلبية قدرة الله قديمة وباقية...إلخ، وكذا يقال في علم الله وغيره من المعاني إلى آخره. وإنما وصفت الصفات بالسلبية لأنها عدمية لا وجود لها في الخارج وأما المعاني فإنها وجودية والمعنوية ملازمة لها فيلزم بها التسلسل ولذا فإن الصفة لا تتصف بصفات المعاني ولا المعنوية. والله جل وعز يجب عقلا ونقلا اتصافه بصفات المعاني والمعنوية فليس هو إذن صفة. بل هو ذات لا تشبه الصفات. إذن هو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى المحل وهو المطلوب. وبرهان الجزء الثاني وهو استغناؤه عن المخصص أنه لو احتاج إلى مخصص فاعل لكان حادثاً ، لأنه لا يحتاج إلى المخصص إلا الحادث، وأن يكون حادثا باطل، لما تقدم من قيام الدليل القاطع على وجوب قدمه تعالى وبقائه.
Proof of the necessity of God's self-subsistence: Self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) means independence from a substratum and a specifier by essence. Thus self-subsistence is independence by essence from substratum and specifier. Therefore self-subsistence is composed of two parts: And this hadith was opposed by Ibn Taymiyya, despite its numerous chains that establish that God was and nothing else existed besides Him, and he claimed that there is no indication of that from the hadith, and he rejected all the wordings except 'God was and nothing was before Him', with flimsy arguments, and that is a distortion from him of the hadith to enable him to assert the generic pre-eternity of the world and to build his anthropomorphism. We have detailed that in our commentary on al-Ikhmīmī's refutation of him and in our book 'al-Kāshif al-Ṣaghīr ʿan ʿAqāʾid Ibn Taymiyya'. Imām Aḥmad narrated in his Musnad from al-Muḥarrar from Abū Hurayra from his father who said: The Messenger of God said: 'People will keep asking until they say: God was before everything, so what was before Him?' The meaning of that is that the question about the beginning of every created thing is valid and has an answer because every created thing has a beginning, but the question about the beginning of the existence of God is invalid because He has no beginning. Rather, He exists before everything. And ponder this narration which explicitly nullifies the infinite regress in pre-eternity and definitively indicates it. And Ibn Taymiyya, as we mentioned, denied the discontinuity of the chain of accidents in pre-eternity, thereby contradicting these narrations and the many verses that indicate that God created all creatures and gave them a beginning. Therefore it is impossible for an infinite chain of creatures to exist. Ibn Taymiyya built most of his doctrine on this false principle. Independence from a substratum and independence from a specifier. As for the proof of the first part: because if He needed a substratum, i.e., an essence in which He inheres like an attribute inheres in the one described, He would be an attribute, and an attribute is a meaning among meanings, and nothing needs a substratum except attributes, and an essence does not need an essence to subsist in it because if an essence could accept to subsist in another essence, then that other essence would subsist in an essence, leading to infinite regress, because acceptance is a self-attribute of every essence, and an attribute is not qualified by attributes of meanings (maʿānī) nor by attributes of meaning-properties (maʿnawiyya) because if an attribute could accept another attribute to subsist in it, its attribute would be likewise, leading to infinite regress and the entry of an infinite into existence. Note: The concept of meanings and meaning-properties is that attributes are qualified by self-attributes and negative attributes, such as saying 'God's knowledge exists', for example; this is a self-attribute. Similarly for other attributes of meanings, the existence of each attribute is intrinsic to it. And one says in describing attributes by negative [attributes]: 'God's power is pre-eternal and perpetual', etc. Likewise it is said of God's knowledge and other meanings, etc. And attributes are described as negative only because they are non-existent, having no external existence. As for meanings, they are existent, and meaning-properties are concomitant with them, leading to infinite regress. Therefore an attribute is not qualified by attributes of meanings or meaning-properties. And God, glorified and mighty, must, rationally and scripturally, be qualified by attributes of meanings and meaning-properties, so He is not an attribute. Rather, He is an essence unlike attributes. Therefore He, exalted, does not need a substratum; that is what was sought. And the proof of the second part, which is His independence from a specifier: because if He needed an efficient specifier, He would be temporal, because nothing needs a specifier except the temporal, and that He be temporal is false due to what preceded of the decisive proof of the necessity of His pre-existence and subsistence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs). It begins by defining self-subsistence as independence from both a substratum (maḥall) and a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The author then inserts a lengthy refutation of Ibn Taymiyya, who denied the hadith that establishes God's precedence over all things and argued for the generic pre-eternity of the world. The author rebuts this using the hadith and verses. After the digression, the proof resumes with two parts. The first part proves that God does not need a substratum: if He did, He would be an attribute, leading to infinite regress. A note clarifies that attributes are not qualified by meanings or meaning-properties, whereas God is, so He cannot be an attribute. The second part proves that God does not need a specifier: that would imply temporality, which contradicts His proven pre-eternity. Thus God is self-subsistent.

Key Terms

محل
مخصص
استغناء
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Taymiyya believed the chain of temporal events is infinite in the past (generic pre-eternity of the world).
Correction: The hadith and reason prove that every creature has a beginning, so there cannot be an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What does self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs) mean according to Sanusi?
A) God's existence is necessary
B) Independence from a substratum and a specifier
C) God is eternal
D) God has no beginning

Key Takeaway
Self-subsistence is composed of two independent concepts: not needing a place and not needing a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The hadith says people will keep asking until they say 'God was before everything, so what was before Him?' Are you content to stop at the boundary of revelation, or do you find yourself seeking to go beyond? What does your curiosity about the unseen reveal about your trust in God's knowledge?

The Two Rational Proofs for God's Independence
Sanusi proves each component of self-subsistence with logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments proving God's independence from a substratum and from a specifier.

Core Idea
If God needed a substratum or specifier, He would be an attribute or temporal – both impossible.

Having defined self-subsistence, Sanusi now provides two separate rational proofs, one for each component. The first proof uses the nature of attributes and infinite regress; the second relies on God's necessary pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof (substratum)
Read the argument that if God needed a substratum, He would be an attribute, leading to infinite regress.
2
Understand the note on attributes
Sanusi clarifies that attributes do not have attributes of meanings or meaning-properties, whereas God does, so He cannot be an attribute.
This reinforces the proof.
3
Read the second proof (specifier)
Read the argument that needing a specifier would make God temporal, which contradicts His necessary pre-eternity.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Both proofs together establish that God is self-subsistent, independent of any external cause or substratum.

برهان وجوب قيام الله بنفسه: والقيام بالنفس معناه الغنى عن المحل والمخصص بالذات، فالقيام بالنفس هو الاستغناء بالذات عن المحل والمخصص، فالقيام بالنفس إذن مركب من جزأين : وهذا الحديث خالف ابن تيمية بن أبي هريرة عن أبيه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : "لا يزال الناس يسألون حتى يقولوا كان الله قبل كل شيء فما كان قبله". والمراد من ذلك أن السؤال عن بداية كل مخلوق سؤال صحيح، وله جواب لأنه يوجد لكل المخلوقات بداية، وأما السؤال عن بداية لوجود الله تعالى فهو سؤال باطل لأنه لا توجد له جل شأنه بداية. بل هو ثابت قبل كل شيء، وتأمل في هذه الرواية التي تنص على إبطال التسلسل في القدم وتدل عليه دلالة أكيدة، وقد قال ابن تيمية كما ذكرنا بعدم انقطاع سلسلة الحوادث في القدم، مخالفا في قوله ذلك هذه الروايات والآيات الكثيرة التي تدل على أن الله تعالى أوجد الخلق كلهم وجعل لهم بداية، وعليه فيستحيل أن توجد سلسلة من المخلوقات لا بداية لها. وقد بنى ابن تيمية أغلب مذهبه على هذه القاعدة الباطلة. الاستغناء عن المحل والاستغناء عن المخصص. فأما برهان الجزء الأول فلأنه تعالى لو احتاج إلى محل أي ذات يحل فيها كما تحل الصفة في الموصوف، لكان صفة، والصفة معنى من المعاني، فلا يحتاج إلى محل إلا الصفات، والذات لا تحتاج إلى ذات تقوم بها لأن الذات لو قبلت أن تقوم بذات أخرى لزم أن الذات الأخرى تقوم بذات فيلزم التسلسل، لأن القبول وصف نفسي لكل ذات والصفة لا تتصف بصفات المعاني ولا المعنوية لأن الضفة لو قبلت أن تقوم بها صفة لكانت صفتها كذلك، فيلزم التسلسل ودخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود. ملاحظة : مفهوم المعاني والمعنوية أن الصفات تتصف بالصفات النفسية والسلبية بأن يقال علم الله موجود مثلاً فهذه صفة نفسية وكذا غير العلم من صفات المعاني فوجود كل صفة منها نفسي لها ويقال في وصف الصفات بالسلبية قدرة الله قديمة وباقية...إلخ، وكذا يقال في علم الله وغيره من المعاني إلى آخره. وإنما وصفت الصفات بالسلبية لأنها عدمية لا وجود لها في الخارج وأما المعاني فإنها وجودية والمعنوية ملازمة لها فيلزم بها التسلسل ولذا فإن الصفة لا تتصف بصفات المعاني ولا المعنوية. والله جل وعز يجب عقلا ونقلا اتصافه بصفات المعاني والمعنوية فليس هو إذن صفة. بل هو ذات لا تشبه الصفات. إذن هو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى المحل وهو المطلوب. وبرهان الجزء الثاني وهو استغناؤه عن المخصص أنه لو احتاج إلى مخصص فاعل لكان حادثاً ، لأنه لا يحتاج إلى المخصص إلا الحادث، وأن يكون حادثا باطل، لما تقدم من قيام الدليل القاطع على وجوب قدمه تعالى وبقائه.
Proof of the necessity of God's self-subsistence: Self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) means independence from a substratum and a specifier by essence. Thus self-subsistence is independence by essence from substratum and specifier. Therefore self-subsistence is composed of two parts: And this hadith was opposed by Ibn Taymiyya, despite its numerous chains that establish that God was and nothing else existed besides Him, and he claimed that there is no indication of that from the hadith, and he rejected all the wordings except 'God was and nothing was before Him', with flimsy arguments, and that is a distortion from him of the hadith to enable him to assert the generic pre-eternity of the world and to build his anthropomorphism. We have detailed that in our commentary on al-Ikhmīmī's refutation of him and in our book 'al-Kāshif al-Ṣaghīr ʿan ʿAqāʾid Ibn Taymiyya'. Imām Aḥmad narrated in his Musnad from al-Muḥarrar from Abū Hurayra from his father who said: The Messenger of God said: 'People will keep asking until they say: God was before everything, so what was before Him?' The meaning of that is that the question about the beginning of every created thing is valid and has an answer because every created thing has a beginning, but the question about the beginning of the existence of God is invalid because He has no beginning. Rather, He exists before everything. And ponder this narration which explicitly nullifies the infinite regress in pre-eternity and definitively indicates it. And Ibn Taymiyya, as we mentioned, denied the discontinuity of the chain of accidents in pre-eternity, thereby contradicting these narrations and the many verses that indicate that God created all creatures and gave them a beginning. Therefore it is impossible for an infinite chain of creatures to exist. Ibn Taymiyya built most of his doctrine on this false principle. Independence from a substratum and independence from a specifier. As for the proof of the first part: because if He needed a substratum, i.e., an essence in which He inheres like an attribute inheres in the one described, He would be an attribute, and an attribute is a meaning among meanings, and nothing needs a substratum except attributes, and an essence does not need an essence to subsist in it because if an essence could accept to subsist in another essence, then that other essence would subsist in an essence, leading to infinite regress, because acceptance is a self-attribute of every essence, and an attribute is not qualified by attributes of meanings (maʿānī) nor by attributes of meaning-properties (maʿnawiyya) because if an attribute could accept another attribute to subsist in it, its attribute would be likewise, leading to infinite regress and the entry of an infinite into existence. Note: The concept of meanings and meaning-properties is that attributes are qualified by self-attributes and negative attributes, such as saying 'God's knowledge exists', for example; this is a self-attribute. Similarly for other attributes of meanings, the existence of each attribute is intrinsic to it. And one says in describing attributes by negative [attributes]: 'God's power is pre-eternal and perpetual', etc. Likewise it is said of God's knowledge and other meanings, etc. And attributes are described as negative only because they are non-existent, having no external existence. As for meanings, they are existent, and meaning-properties are concomitant with them, leading to infinite regress. Therefore an attribute is not qualified by attributes of meanings or meaning-properties. And God, glorified and mighty, must, rationally and scripturally, be qualified by attributes of meanings and meaning-properties, so He is not an attribute. Rather, He is an essence unlike attributes. Therefore He, exalted, does not need a substratum; that is what was sought. And the proof of the second part, which is His independence from a specifier: because if He needed an efficient specifier, He would be temporal, because nothing needs a specifier except the temporal, and that He be temporal is false due to what preceded of the decisive proof of the necessity of His pre-existence and subsistence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs). It begins by defining self-subsistence as independence from both a substratum (maḥall) and a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The author then inserts a lengthy refutation of Ibn Taymiyya, who denied the hadith that establishes God's precedence over all things and argued for the generic pre-eternity of the world. The author rebuts this using the hadith and verses. After the digression, the proof resumes with two parts. The first part proves that God does not need a substratum: if He did, He would be an attribute, leading to infinite regress. A note clarifies that attributes are not qualified by meanings or meaning-properties, whereas God is, so He cannot be an attribute. The second part proves that God does not need a specifier: that would imply temporality, which contradicts His proven pre-eternity. Thus God is self-subsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does knowing that God is not subject to change or specification reshape your trust in His promises and His plan for you?

The Four Types of Tawheed
Sanusi enumerates the four divisions of tawheed with brief definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of tawheed (divinity, acts, attributes, and essence) and their distinct meanings in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Tawheed is not monolithic; it has four essential dimensions that collectively define God's oneness.

Sanusi opens this passage by classifying tawheed into four kinds, providing a clear conceptual framework that underpins the rational proof to follow. Understanding these categories is essential before engaging with the argument for God's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the four divisions of tawheed. Notice the concise definitions Sanusi provides for each type.
The four types are: uluhiyya, af'al, sifat, and dhat.
2
Understand each type
For each type, identify the core claim: tawheed of divinity (God alone is worthy of worship), of acts (God alone is creator), of attributes (God alone possesses true attributes), of essence (God alone exists necessarily).
3
Connect to the proof
Consider why Sanusi begins with classification before launching into the proof. This framework sets the stage for arguing that God's oneness must be comprehensive.
The proof will show that any failure in oneness leads to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب الوحدانية الله. التوحيد على أقسام منها : توحيد الألوهية وتوحيد الأفعال وتوحيد الصفات وتوحيد الذات وتوحيد الألوهية مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الإله وحده، أي هو المتفرد بوصف الألوهية الذي يرجع كل ممكن لاحتياجه إليه، وتوحيد الأفعال مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الفاعل وحده ، أي أنَّ الله هو الخالق وَحْدَه وتوحيد الصفات مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الحي وحده، وتوحيد الذات مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الموجود على الحقية (١) . والدليل على الوحدانية أن نقول : لو لم يكن واحداً في ذاته وفي صفاته وفي أفعاله لزم أن لا يوجد شيء من العالم أجراماً كانت أو أعراضاً للزوم عجزه حينئذ. وبيان الدليل كما يلي : لو كان معه مماثل في الألوهية للزم عجزهما سواء اتفقا أو اختلفا أو اقتسما، فكل من هذه الأقسام، إما أن يكون اضطرارياً أو اختيارياً ، فإن كان اضطرارياً لزم قهرهما وعجزهما فينتفي العالم ونفي العالم محال. وإن اتفقا اختيارا فيتعرض لهما جوهر فرد فإن أوجداه معا لزم انقسام ما لا ينقسم ) وهو محال، لأن الجوهر الفرد لا يقبل الانقسام وإن أوجد أحدهما عين ما أوجده الآخر لزم تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال، وإن أوجده أحدهما وعجز الآخر، فالآخر ليس بإله لأنه عجز والإله ليس بعاجز، والذي قدر على فعله أيضاً ليس بإله لأن الفرض أنه مثل للآخر، ومثل العاجز عاجز. وإن عجز الإثنان فهما ليسا بإلهين، وإذا لزم عجزهما عن إيجاد هذا الجوهر لزم عجزهما عن سائر الممكنات لأنه لا فرق بين ممكن وممكن. وإن اختلفا بأن يريد أحدهما حركة جسم في زمان واحد ويريد الآخر سكونه في ذلك الزمان بعينه، فلا يصح أن يحصل مرادهما لما فيه من الجمع بين الضدين ولا يصح عدم مرادهما لما فيه من ارتفاع الضدين مع قبول الجسم لهما، ولا يصح أن يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لأن عجز أحدهما دليل على عجز الآخر لأنه مثله ، وإن اقتسما اختياراً لزم عجز كل واحد منهما
Proof of the necessity of God's oneness (waḥdāniyya). Tawḥīd has divisions: tawḥīd of divinity (ulūhiyya), tawḥīd of acts (afʿāl), tawḥīd of attributes (ṣifāt), and tawḥīd of essence (dhāt). Tawḥīd of divinity is that God is the deity alone, i.e., He is unique in the description of divinity, to which every possible being is referred due to its need of Him. Tawḥīd of acts is that God is the agent alone, i.e., that God is the sole creator. Tawḥīd of attributes is that God is the living one alone. Tawḥīd of essence is that God is the existent in reality (ʿalā al-ḥaqīqa). The proof for oneness is that we say: If He were not one in His essence, attributes, and acts, it would follow that nothing of the world exists, whether bodies or accidents, because of His necessary incapacity then. The explanation of the proof is as follows: If there were with Him a similar one in divinity, then their incapacity would follow, whether they agreed, disagreed, or divided. Each of these divisions is either coerced or voluntary. If coerced, their being compelled and incapable follows, so the world would be nonexistent, and the nonexistence of the world is impossible. If they voluntarily agreed, then an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) would be presented to them. If they brought it into existence together, it would necessitate the division of what is indivisible – which is impossible, because the indivisible atom does not accept division. If one of them brought into existence exactly what the other brought, it would necessitate the attainment of the attained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. If one brought it into existence and the other was incapable, then the other is not a god because he is incapable, and the god is not incapable, and the one who was able to do it is also not a god because the assumption is that he is similar to the other, and the similar of the incapable is incapable. If both are incapable, then both are not gods. And if their incapacity to bring into existence this atom is necessary, then their incapacity to bring into existence all other possibles follows, because there is no distinction between one possible and another. If they disagreed, e.g., one wants the motion of a body at a certain time and the other wants its rest at that same time, it is not sound that both their desires occur because that would combine opposites, nor that neither desire occurs because that would remove opposites while the body is receptive to both, nor that only one's desire occurs because the incapacity of one indicates the incapacity of the other due to their similarity. If they voluntarily divided (the world), then the incapacity of each one of them follows.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of tawḥīd and a rational proof for the oneness of God. It first divides tawḥīd into four kinds: tawḥīd of divinity (God alone is worthy of worship), of acts (God alone is the creator), of attributes (God alone possesses attributes like life in a unique way), and of essence (God alone is truly existent by Himself, not deriving existence from another). The proof then argues that if there were two gods, they would necessarily be incapable, which is impossible. It explores three scenarios: agreement, disagreement, and division. Under voluntary agreement, their inability to co-create an indivisible atom without logical contradiction (division of the indivisible, or producing what already exists) shows their mutual dependence or incapacity, leading to the conclusion that neither is truly divine. Under disagreement, their conflicting wills would result in logical impossibility (both opposite states cannot occur, nor can neither, nor can one alone without the other being divine). Under division, each would be limited and thus incapable of independent action. Hence, necessarily, there is only one God.

Key Terms
توحيد الألوهية
توحيد الأفعال
توحيد الصفات
توحيد الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Tawheed of essence

Key Takeaway
Tawheed has four necessary dimensions: divinity, acts, attributes, and essence; each must be upheld to affirm God's complete oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof of Oneness – Agreement and Disagreement of Two Gods
Sanusi presents the proof for oneness, focusing on the scenarios of agreement and disagreement between two hypothetical gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hypothetical agreement or disagreement of two gods leads to logical contradictions and impossible outcomes, proving they cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot coexist because any attempted cooperation or conflict results in impossibility.

Sanusi now presents the detailed rational proof for God's oneness. He starts by showing that if there were two gods, whether they act under coercion or voluntarily, their agreement or disagreement would lead to contradictions—such as dividing an indivisible atom or producing what already exists—proving their incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the overall proof structure
Read the passage from the initial conditional statement through the discussion of agreement and disagreement. Notice the systematic breakdown of possibilities.
Sanusi examines coercion vs. voluntary action, then agreement vs. disagreement.
2
Analyze the coerced scenario
Understand why coerced action would imply incapacity: if both are compelled, they are not truly powerful, so the world would not exist.
Coercion negates the attribute of will, which is essential for divinity.
3
Examine voluntary agreement
Explore the three sub-possibilities under voluntary agreement: creating an indivisible atom together (impossible because it would split the indivisible), one creating what the other already created (impossible because it duplicates), or one creating alone while the other fails (shows incapacity of the other).
The concept of jawhar fard (indivisible atom) is key.
4
Examine voluntary disagreement
Consider the case where they want opposite states for the same body: both outcomes cannot occur, neither can fail (since the body is receptive), and only one succeeding would show the other's incapacity and thus non-divinity.
This shows that disagreement also leads to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب الوحدانية الله. التوحيد على أقسام منها : توحيد الألوهية وتوحيد الأفعال وتوحيد الصفات وتوحيد الذات وتوحيد الألوهية مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الإله وحده، أي هو المتفرد بوصف الألوهية الذي يرجع كل ممكن لاحتياجه إليه، وتوحيد الأفعال مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الفاعل وحده ، أي أنَّ الله هو الخالق وَحْدَه وتوحيد الصفات مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الحي وحده، وتوحيد الذات مرجعه إلى أن الله هو الموجود على الحقية (١) . والدليل على الوحدانية أن نقول : لو لم يكن واحداً في ذاته وفي صفاته وفي أفعاله لزم أن لا يوجد شيء من العالم أجراماً كانت أو أعراضاً للزوم عجزه حينئذ. وبيان الدليل كما يلي : لو كان معه مماثل في الألوهية للزم عجزهما سواء اتفقا أو اختلفا أو اقتسما، فكل من هذه الأقسام، إما أن يكون اضطرارياً أو اختيارياً ، فإن كان اضطرارياً لزم قهرهما وعجزهما فينتفي العالم ونفي العالم محال. وإن اتفقا اختيارا فيتعرض لهما جوهر فرد فإن أوجداه معا لزم انقسام ما لا ينقسم ) وهو محال، لأن الجوهر الفرد لا يقبل الانقسام وإن أوجد أحدهما عين ما أوجده الآخر لزم تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال، وإن أوجده أحدهما وعجز الآخر، فالآخر ليس بإله لأنه عجز والإله ليس بعاجز، والذي قدر على فعله أيضاً ليس بإله لأن الفرض أنه مثل للآخر، ومثل العاجز عاجز. وإن عجز الإثنان فهما ليسا بإلهين، وإذا لزم عجزهما عن إيجاد هذا الجوهر لزم عجزهما عن سائر الممكنات لأنه لا فرق بين ممكن وممكن. وإن اختلفا بأن يريد أحدهما حركة جسم في زمان واحد ويريد الآخر سكونه في ذلك الزمان بعينه، فلا يصح أن يحصل مرادهما لما فيه من الجمع بين الضدين ولا يصح عدم مرادهما لما فيه من ارتفاع الضدين مع قبول الجسم لهما، ولا يصح أن يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لأن عجز أحدهما دليل على عجز الآخر لأنه مثله ، وإن اقتسما اختياراً لزم عجز كل واحد منهما
Proof of the necessity of God's oneness (waḥdāniyya). Tawḥīd has divisions: tawḥīd of divinity (ulūhiyya), tawḥīd of acts (afʿāl), tawḥīd of attributes (ṣifāt), and tawḥīd of essence (dhāt). Tawḥīd of divinity is that God is the deity alone, i.e., He is unique in the description of divinity, to which every possible being is referred due to its need of Him. Tawḥīd of acts is that God is the agent alone, i.e., that God is the sole creator. Tawḥīd of attributes is that God is the living one alone. Tawḥīd of essence is that God is the existent in reality (ʿalā al-ḥaqīqa). The proof for oneness is that we say: If He were not one in His essence, attributes, and acts, it would follow that nothing of the world exists, whether bodies or accidents, because of His necessary incapacity then. The explanation of the proof is as follows: If there were with Him a similar one in divinity, then their incapacity would follow, whether they agreed, disagreed, or divided. Each of these divisions is either coerced or voluntary. If coerced, their being compelled and incapable follows, so the world would be nonexistent, and the nonexistence of the world is impossible. If they voluntarily agreed, then an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) would be presented to them. If they brought it into existence together, it would necessitate the division of what is indivisible – which is impossible, because the indivisible atom does not accept division. If one of them brought into existence exactly what the other brought, it would necessitate the attainment of the attained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. If one brought it into existence and the other was incapable, then the other is not a god because he is incapable, and the god is not incapable, and the one who was able to do it is also not a god because the assumption is that he is similar to the other, and the similar of the incapable is incapable. If both are incapable, then both are not gods. And if their incapacity to bring into existence this atom is necessary, then their incapacity to bring into existence all other possibles follows, because there is no distinction between one possible and another. If they disagreed, e.g., one wants the motion of a body at a certain time and the other wants its rest at that same time, it is not sound that both their desires occur because that would combine opposites, nor that neither desire occurs because that would remove opposites while the body is receptive to both, nor that only one's desire occurs because the incapacity of one indicates the incapacity of the other due to their similarity. If they voluntarily divided (the world), then the incapacity of each one of them follows.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of tawḥīd and a rational proof for the oneness of God. It first divides tawḥīd into four kinds: tawḥīd of divinity (God alone is worthy of worship), of acts (God alone is the creator), of attributes (God alone possesses attributes like life in a unique way), and of essence (God alone is truly existent by Himself, not deriving existence from another). The proof then argues that if there were two gods, they would necessarily be incapable, which is impossible. It explores three scenarios: agreement, disagreement, and division. Under voluntary agreement, their inability to co-create an indivisible atom without logical contradiction (division of the indivisible, or producing what already exists) shows their mutual dependence or incapacity, leading to the conclusion that neither is truly divine. Under disagreement, their conflicting wills would result in logical impossibility (both opposite states cannot occur, nor can neither, nor can one alone without the other being divine). Under division, each would be limited and thus incapable of independent action. Hence, necessarily, there is only one God.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
تحصيل الحاصل
عجز
مثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could simply agree to cooperate without problem.
Correction: Even with agreement, they face logical contradictions such as dividing an indivisible atom or creating the same effect twice (tahsil al-hasil), which are impossible.

Checkpoint
What logical impossibility arises if two gods voluntarily agree to create the same indivisible atom?
A) The atom would be destroyed.
B) The atom would be divided, but it is indivisible.
C) The atom would become a compound.
D) The atom would require a creator.

Key Takeaway
Two gods, whether agreeing or disagreeing, lead to logical impossibilities; therefore, there can only be one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you have placed as a 'second god' in your heart—a desire, a person, a status—that you treat as if it has independent power. After reading this proof, what logical absurdity would such an idol imply about your understanding of Allah's uniqueness? This week, what action will you take to remove that idol's influence?
?
This argument shows that even 'agreement' between two gods leads to impossibility. Reflect on your life: where are you trying to serve both Allah and your desires while claiming they 'agree'? What specific contradiction will you face if you continue? Commit to one change today.

Proof of Oneness – The Division Scenario and Conclusion
Sanusi presents the final scenario of the proof: the division of creation between two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing sovereignty between two gods renders each incapable, thereby completing the rational proof for God's oneness.

Core Idea
If two gods divide creation, each becomes limited and thus not truly divine.

Sanusi concludes the proof by considering the third scenario: the two gods voluntarily divide the world between them. This too leads to each being unable to act outside their domain, implying incapacity and therefore non-divinity. This final argument seals the case that God must be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the division scenario
Read the short passage on division. It states that if the two gods divide the world, each becomes incapable (of the other's domain).
The scenario is brief but powerful.
2
Understand the implication
Reflect on why division implies incapacity: each god would be dependent on the other for the part not under its control, so they are not independent or all-powerful.
True divinity requires absolute power without limitation.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Recall the earlier scenarios (coerced, agreement, disagreement). The division scenario is the final possibility, all of which lead to impossibility, so the only valid conclusion is that God is one.
The proof is a complete disjunction of all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Proof of Divine Unity and Refutation of an Objection
Sanusi explains the proof of divine unity (burhān al-tamānuʿ) and refutes an objection regarding cooperative human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of divine unity known as burhān al-tamānuʿ and how it refutes the objection that cooperative human acts undermine the argument.

Core Idea
The proof of tamānuʿ shows that two independent creators cannot coexist because creation is origination from nothing, not energy transfer.

Sanusi presents the decisive proof of God's oneness by demonstrating that if there were two creators, their wills could conflict, leading to impossibility. He then addresses a common objection involving cooperative acts, clarifying that creation is fundamentally different from transferring energy. This lesson unpacks the logic of the proof and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for a deity
Read the opening statement: the condition for a deity is that His power must relate to all possibles. This sets the stage for the proof.
Note that this condition is essential for the argument.
2
Examine the proof of mutual prevention
Analyze Sanusi's reasoning: if two creators exist, they could will contradictory things, leading to absurdity. Note that this applies to any creator, not just deities.
This is the core of burhān al-tamānuʿ.
3
Consider the objection about cooperative acts
Read the objection: two men can lift a stone together, so why not two creators? Identify the core assumption behind this objection.
The objection seems plausible but rests on a misunderstanding.
4
Learn the refutation
Study how Sanusi refutes the objection by distinguishing creation (origination from nothing) from energy transfer. Also understand why another attempted answer is invalid.
Focus on the idea that creation is ibdāʿ min ʿadam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما عند صاحبه لأن شرط الإله يجب أن تكون قدرته عامة التعلق بجميع الممكنات. ومن هذا البرهان تعلم أن الإله واحد وهو برهان قاطع في ذلك. ملاحظة: من برهان الوحدانية يلزم أنه لا تأثير للعباد بقدرتهم الحادثة لا مباشرة (١) ولا تولداً ، بل لهم كسب فقط فهو مقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور من غير تأثير. هذا لا برد [؟] دليل التمانع يستفاد منه عموم تعلق القدرة الإلهية بالممكنات كما سبق ، وامتناع وجود خالق غير الله تعالى ، سواءً كان إلهاً آخر أم غير إله ، والخلق هو الإبراز من العدم لا تفاوت فيه حتى يقال إن الخالق الأول أقوى من الثاني ، بل إذا جوزنا الخلق على الثاني فَنُجَوِّزُهُ بنفس المستوى الجائز في حق الأول ، ولذلك فلا تفاوت في تعلق قدرة الخلق بالمخلوقِ سواءً كان القادر إلهاً أو فرضناه مخلوقاً فإذا دلّنا دليل التمانع على نفي إله آخر فلانًا فرضناه قادراً على الخلق ، وهذا يوجب إمكان التمانع المستلزم للمحال ، وهو يوجب نفي خالق ثان من غير تقييده بكونه إلها أو أنساناً . وقد أورد بعضهم على برهان الوحدانية بأن فيه أنه يجوز اجتماع نحو رجلين على رفع حجر ، ولكن مطلقاً لأن الطاقة الصادرة من كل واحد منهما متعلقة بما لا يتعلق به الآخر ، والتأثير المتعارض منهما يُلْغَى بالآخر فهذه الصورة ليست واردة على برهان التوحيد . وأيضاً فهذا التمثيل يقوم على فهم مغلوط للخلق ، فالمثال أساسه أن الإنسان عندما يحمل حجراً فإن ما يفعله هو إكساب الحجر بعضاً من طاقته ، فهو نقل للطاقة من محل إلى محل ، وأما الخلق فليس الأمر فيه كذلك ، فلا نقل فيها من محل إلى آخر ، بل هو إبداع من عدم . أما الجواب على الإيراد بأن هذا ناتج عن عدم التفريق بين القدرة الكاملة والقدرة الحادثة القابلة للزيادة والنقصان ، فهو جواب باطل ، لأن الوصف بالكمال والاستقلال معناه تمام التأثير وكفايته وأما اللاتناهي فهو عموم التعلق ولا دخل له هنا ، والمعتبر في هذا المثال إنما هو الوصف الأول ولا مدخلية للثاني ، ولا فرق بين قدرة الله تعالى وقدرة غيره في التأثير إذا فرضت خالقه ، وهو الفرض محل الكلام ، فكلاهما قدرة مستقلة في خلق وإحداث الأثر المطلوب ، كما هو مُدَّعَي الخصم فيمكن حصول التعارض بينهما إذا سلم هذا القدر ، وعليه فيجري برهان التمانع ، فيكفي فرض تمام التأثير في القدرة واشتراك المحل المتأثر لجواز فرض حصول التمانع والتعارض لإقامة برهان التمانع.
From his companion: because the condition of the deity is that His power must be universally related to all possibles. And from this proof you learn that the deity is one, and it is a decisive proof for that. Note: from the proof of unity it follows that servants have no effect by their originated power, neither directly nor by generation, but they have only acquisition (kasb), which is the accompaniment of the originated power to the act without effect. This is not refuted [؟]. The proof of mutual prevention (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) derives from it the universal relation of divine power to possibles, as previously mentioned, and the impossibility of the existence of a creator other than Allah, whether another deity or non-deity. Creation is bringing forth from non-existence; there is no variation in it such that one could say the first creator is stronger than the second. Rather, if we permit creation to the second, we permit it at the same level permissible for the first. Therefore, there is no variation in the relation of the power of creation to the created, whether the powerful one is a deity or we suppose it created. So if the proof of mutual prevention indicates the negation of another deity, it is because we suppose it capable of creation, and this necessitates the possibility of mutual prevention, which entails an impossibility, and it necessitates the negation of a second creator without restricting it to being a deity or a human. Some have objected to the proof of unity saying that it allows for the cooperation of two men in lifting a stone, but absolutely, because the energy emanating from each of them relates to what the other does not relate to, and the conflicting effect of each is nullified by the other. Thus, this example does not apply to the proof of divine unity. Also, this example is based on a mistaken understanding of creation, for the example is founded on the fact that when a man lifts a stone, what he does is impart some of his energy to the stone, so it is a transfer of energy from one place to another. As for creation, it is not like that; there is no transfer from one place to another, but it is origination from nothing. As for the answer to the objection that this results from not distinguishing between complete power and originated power subject to increase and decrease, it is an invalid answer, because the description of completeness and independence means complete and sufficient effect; as for infinitude, it is universal relation and has no bearing here. What matters in this example is only the first description, and the second has no relevance. There is no difference between the power of Allah and the power of another in effect if it is supposed to be a creator, and this is the supposition under discussion, so both are independent powers in creating and producing the desired effect, as the opponent claims. Thus, conflict between them can occur if this is conceded, and accordingly the proof of mutual prevention applies. It suffices to suppose complete effect in power and the sharing of the affected locus to permit the supposition of mutual prevention and conflict to establish the proof of mutual prevention.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage explains and defends the proof of divine unity (burhān al-tamānuʿ) and refutes an objection. It begins by stating that the condition for being a deity is that power must extend to all possible things, and from this proof one knows that God is one. A note adds that from this proof it follows that humans have no effect by their acquired power; they only have kasb (acquisition), which is the accompaniment of power to the act without causal influence. Then the text presents the proof of mutual prevention in detail: it shows that if there were two creators, each could will conflicting things, leading to impossibility. The proof operates regardless of whether the second creator is a deity or a human. An objection is raised: two men can cooperate in lifting a stone, so why can't two creators coexist? The text refutes this by pointing out that the example involves transfer of energy, whereas creation is origination from nothing, not transfer. Another attempted answer—distinguishing between complete power (divine) and limited power (human)—is rejected because the proof does not depend on infinitude but on complete effectiveness. If both powers are supposed to be independent and effective, conflict is possible, and the proof stands. The passage concludes by reiterating the sufficiency of the proof.

Key Terms
برهان التمانع
وحدانية
قدرة
كسب
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of tamānuʿ fails because two beings can cooperate, as shown by two men lifting a stone.
Correction: The example of lifting a stone involves transfer of energy, whereas creation is origination from nothing. Therefore, the analogy does not apply, and the proof stands.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof of tamānuʿ refute the example of two men lifting a stone?
A) Because the men are not gods.
B) Because the example involves transfer of energy, not origination from nothing.
C) Because the men have limited power.
D) Because the stone is not a possible thing.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual prevention (burhān al-tamānuʿ) decisively establishes God's oneness by showing that two independent creators lead to contradiction, and superficial objections based on human cooperation are invalid because creation is origination from nothing.

What would it take for you to genuinely feel that your successes are not your own doing?
?
Sanusi emphasizes that creation is bringing forth from nothing, not a transfer of energy. Consider something you have 'created' recently—a work of art, a solution to a problem, a relationship. How much of it was truly your own origination from nothing, and how much was just rearranging what Allah had already provided? What does this teach you about your dependence on Him?
