Proof for the Permissibility of God's Actions on Possible Things
Sanusi proves that God's actions on possible things are neither necessary nor impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's actions regarding possible things are purely permissible (ja'iz), not necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
If any possible action were necessary or impossible for God, all would be—contradiction.

Sanusi now demonstrates that God's actions on possible things cannot be rationally necessary or impossible; they must be purely permissible. The proof uses a simple but powerful logical argument: if even one possible thing were necessary for God, then all possible things would become necessary, which contradicts their very nature as possible. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the complete Arabic passage once without focusing on details. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the logical flow.
The passage includes a note at the end; include it in your reading.
2
Understand the concept of 'possible' (mumkin)
Recall that a possible thing is defined as something whose existence and non-existence are both rationally conceivable. This is key to the proof.
If something is possible, it accepts both existence and non-existence.
3
Follow the hypothetical scenario
The proof considers: what if a single possible action were rationally necessary for God? Then that action would only accept existence, contradicting its possibility. Similarly for impossibility.
The author argues that if one possible becomes necessary, all must become necessary, because there is no distinction among possible things.
4
Recognize the contradiction
A possible thing cannot simultaneously accept both existence and non-existence (by definition) and only accept one. This is a logical contradiction, so the hypothesis is false.
The contradiction is between 'accepts both' and 'accepts only one'.
5
Conclude the permissibility
Since necessity and impossibility are ruled out, the only remaining rational judgment for all possible things regarding God's action is permissibility (ja'iz).
This proof establishes that God is free to act or refrain regarding all possible things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان جواز فعل الممكنات وتركها : لو وجب عليه تعالى فعل شيء من الممكنات عقلاً أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب الممكن الذي صح في العقل وجوده وعدمه واجباً لا يتصور في العقل عدمه، فلو وجب عليه بعض الممكنات عقلاً لزم أن تنقلب كلها واجبة أو استحال بعضها عقلاً لزم أن تنقلب كلها مستحيلة وذلك باطل لا يتصور في العقل وجوده لأنه يؤدي إلى الجمع بين النقيضين لأنه بالنظر لكونه ممكناً يقبل الوجود والعدم وبالنظر لكونه واجباً لا يقبل إلا الوجود فقط، وبالنظر لكونه مستحيلا لا يقبل إلا العدم فقط، وكون الشيء الواحد يقبل الأمرين ولا يقبل إلا واحداً تناقض وهو باطل. ملاحظة : إذا وجب عليه البعض وجب الكل، وإذا استحال البعض استحال الكل لأنه لا فرق بين ممكن وممكن لأنه ما ثبت للأمر ثبت لمثيله.
The proof for the permissibility of acting on possible things and refraining from them: If an action among possible things were rationally necessary for Him, exalted, or rationally impossible, then the possible thing, whose existence and non-existence are rationally sound, would turn into something necessary whose non-existence is not conceivable in reason. So if some possible things were rationally necessary for Him, then all of them would necessarily become necessary; or if some of them were rationally impossible, then all of them would necessarily become impossible. That is false, not conceivable in reason, because it leads to the conjunction of two opposites. For with respect to its being possible, it accepts existence and non-existence; with respect to its being necessary, it accepts only existence; and with respect to its being impossible, it accepts only non-existence. That one thing should accept both matters and yet accept only one is a contradiction, and that is false. Note: If some are necessary for Him, then all are necessary; and if some are impossible, then all are impossible, because there is no difference between one possible thing and another, because what has been established for a matter is established for its like.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof that God's actions with respect to possible things are purely permissible (ja'iz), meaning He may do them or refrain from them without any compulsion or prohibition. The author argues that if any particular possible action were rationally necessary or rationally impossible for God, then all possible actions would share that status, leading to a contradiction. Specifically, if a possible thing became necessary, it would no longer be possible (since it would only accept existence), and if it became impossible, it would no longer be possible (since it would only accept non-existence). This contradicts its original nature as a possible thing, which by definition accepts both existence and non-existence. The author adds a note: if one possible becomes necessary, all become necessary; if one becomes impossible, all become impossible, because there is no differentiating factor among possible things. Since it is evident that not all possible things are necessary or impossible for God (e.g., He created the world but could have not created it), the only remaining rational position is that all possible things are equally permissible for God.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's wisdom or will can make certain possible actions necessary or impossible for Him.
Correction: The proof shows that if any possible action were necessary or impossible, all would be, which contradicts the very nature of possibility and observable reality. Thus, all possible actions remain purely permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if one possible thing became rationally necessary for God?
A) Only that specific thing would be necessary, others remain possible
B) All possible things would become necessary
C) The thing would no longer be possible, but others would be unaffected
D) God would be compelled to act, but His freedom is not affected

Key Takeaway
God's actions on possible things are purely permissible because necessity or impossibility would lead to a logical contradiction, destroying the very nature of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something, do you ever assume that God must answer your prayer in a specific way? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your reliance on God if you truly believed His actions on possible things are purely permissible?

Think of a time you were certain about how God should act—maybe regarding a trial or a blessing. In light of this proof, how might your certainty have been a hidden form of claiming to know what is necessary for God? What inner shift does this demand of you?
?
If God's actions on all possible things are equally permissible, then your current situation—whether easy or difficult—is purely from His will, not from necessity. Does your heart truly accept that, or do you still feel entitled to something different? What would it take for you to surrender that feeling?

Allah's Self-Sufficiency Entails Necessary Existence and Pre-Eternity
Sanusi shows how self-sufficiency entails necessary existence and pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's self-sufficiency (istighna') necessarily entails both necessary existence (wujub al-wujud) and pre-eternity (qidam), and how reason and revelation confirm this.

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency entails necessity and pre-eternity – a being that needs nothing cannot be contingent.

In this passage, Sanusi unpacks the meaning of divinity and shows that Allah's self-sufficiency is not a passive attribute but one that logically implies two fundamental divine attributes: necessary existence and pre-eternity. By establishing that Allah is self-sufficient, we can deduce that He must exist necessarily and cannot have come into being. This lesson traces that logical entailment, supported by both rational proof and Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of divinity by correspondence
Read the opening lines where Sanusi defines divinity as necessary existence and deserving of worship. Notice how the universal concept of 'god' is confined to Allah alone.
This sets the stage for the attributes derived from self-sufficiency.
2
Identify the two entailments from self-sufficiency
The passage states that self-sufficiency necessitates necessary existence and pre-eternity. Locate these two claims and see how each is argued.
These are the core doctrinal points of the lesson.
3
Examine the rational proof for necessary existence
Sanusi uses the proof of self-subsistence (burhan al-qiyam bi al-nafs) to argue that if Allah were possible, He would need a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Understand this logical chain.
This is a key aqli (rational) proof in kalam.
4
Connect to the Quranic evidence
Read verse 35:15 which declares Allah as the Self-Sufficient and mankind as needy. See how revelation supports the rational conclusion.
Both reason and revelation are used together.
5
Trace the entailment of pre-eternity
Follow the argument that if Allah were not pre-eternal, He would be originated and thus need an originator, again negating self-sufficiency. This shows qidam is inseparable from istighna'.
Pre-eternity is a necessary consequence, not a separate addition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معنى الألوهية مطابقة فهو عبارة عن وجوب الوجود واستحقاق العبادة ومعنى الإله كلي انحصر بالأدلة النقلية والعقلية في فرد واحد ولا شك أن هذا المعنى خاص بالله تعالى فيكون لا إله إلا الله لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه ومفتقراً إليه كل ما عداه إلا الله تعالى، أو هو لا إله 'واجب الوجود ومستحق للعبادة إلا الله تعالى إذ هو الإله الواجب الوجود المستحق للعبادة. أما العقائد التي تندرج تحت الاستغناء فهي غناه الذاتي الأبدي جل وعز عن كل ما سواه فاستغناؤه يوجب له تعالى وجوب الوجود لأنه لو كان جائز الوجود لافتقر إلى محدث والافتقار ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً قال الله تعالى ( يَتَأَيُّها النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْغَنِيُّ الْحَمِيدُ ﴾ [فاطر: ١٥) ، وأما العقل فهو برهان القيام بالنفس. ملاحظة : معنى جل هو تنزه عن كل نقص، ومعنى عزّ هو انفرد بكل كمال. و استغناؤه تعالى يوجب له القدم، أي انتفاء العدم السابق للوجود لأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، ولو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحدث، وينتفي عنه الغنى، ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
As for the meaning of divinity by correspondence, it is an expression of necessary existence and deserving of worship. The meaning of 'god' is a universal that has been limited by transmitted and rational proofs to one individual, and there is no doubt that this meaning is specific to Allah. Thus, 'there is no god but Allah' means 'there is no self-sufficient from everything other than Him, and everything other than Him is in need of Him, except Allah,' or it is 'there is no necessary of existence and deserving of worship except Allah,' since He is the God, the Necessary Existence, the Deserving of worship. As for the beliefs that are subsumed under self-sufficiency: they are His essential and eternal self-sufficiency, the Mighty and Majestic, from everything other than Him. His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him necessary existence, because if He were possible of existence, He would be in need of a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency. Negating self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and revelation. Allah said: 'O mankind, you are the ones in need of Allah, while Allah is the Self-Sufficient, the Praiseworthy' (Fatir 15). As for reason, it is the proof of self-subsistence. Note: the meaning of 'Jalla' is being transcendent above every deficiency, and the meaning of 'Azza' is being unique in every perfection. His self-sufficiency necessitates pre-eternity (qidam), i.e., the negation of non-existence preceding existence, because if He were not pre-eternal, He would be originated, and if He were originated, He would be in need of an originator, and self-sufficiency would be negated from Him. Negating self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and revelation.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of divinity by correspondence (mutabaqah) as necessary existence (wujub al-wujud) and deserving of worship (istihqaq al-ibadah). It explains that the universal concept of 'god' is confined to one individual by proofs. The shahada then means either 'no self-sufficient except Allah' or 'no necessary of existence and deserving of worship except Allah.' The author then focuses on the beliefs subsumed under self-sufficiency (istighna'). Self-sufficiency entails necessary existence: if Allah were possible (ja'iz al-wujud), He would need a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Both revelation (Quran 35:15) and reason (the proof of self-subsistence) affirm His self-sufficiency. A note explains 'Jalla' as transcendence above deficiency and 'Azza' as uniqueness in perfection. Self-sufficiency also entails pre-eternity (qidam): if not pre-eternal, He would be originated, needing an originator, negating self-sufficiency. Again, reason and revelation confirm this.

استحقاق العبادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think self-sufficiency only means no need for others, but it implies necessary existence and pre-eternity.
Correction: Self-sufficiency is not a passive attribute; it logically necessitates that the self-sufficient being cannot be contingent or originated, so it must be necessary and pre-eternal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-sufficiency is a foundational attribute from which necessary existence and pre-eternity are logically derived.

Self-Sufficiency Proves Necessary Existence
Sanusi derives necessary existence from self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's absolute self-sufficiency logically entails that His existence is necessary, not contingent.

Core Idea
If Allah needed a creator, He would not be self-sufficient; but He is, so He must exist necessarily.

This passage begins by stating the beliefs that flow from Allah's attribute of self-sufficiency. The author then argues that self-sufficiency directly implies the necessity of existence, because a contingent being would require a cause, which contradicts self-sufficiency. Both reason and revelation confirm this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms: الاستغناء, وجوب الوجود, القيام بالنفس.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if-then.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is: Self-sufficiency is established → if contingent, need a creator → contradiction → thus necessary existence.
The rational proof is called 'proof of self-subsistence'.
3
Reflect on the note
The author explains the names 'Jall' and 'Azza' as transcendence and uniqueness. How do these relate to self-sufficiency?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العقائد التي تندرج تحت الاستغناء فهي غناه الذاتي الأبدي جل وعز عن كل ما سواه فاستغناؤه يوجب له تعالى وجوب الوجود لأنه لو كان جائز الوجود لافتقر إلى محدث والافتقار ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً قال الله تعالى ( يَتَأَيُّها النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْغَنِيُّ الْحَمِيدُ ﴾ [فاطر: ١٥) ، وأما العقل فهو برهان القيام بالنفس. ملاحظة : معنى جل هو تنزه عن كل نقص، ومعنى عزّ هو انفرد بكل كمال. و استغناؤه تعالى يوجب له القدم، أي انتفاء العدم السابق للوجود لأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، ولو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحدث، وينتفي عنه الغنى، ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
As for the beliefs that fall under self-sufficiency (al-istighna), they are His essential and eternal self-sufficiency, Majestic and Mighty, above everything other than Him. So His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud), because if He were contingent in existence (ja'iz al-wujud), He would need a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission. Allah said: 'O mankind, you are the poor toward Allah, and Allah is the Self-Sufficient, the Praiseworthy.' [Fatir:15]. As for reason, it is the proof of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). Note: The meaning of 'Jall' is His transcendence above every deficiency, and the meaning of 'Azza' is His uniqueness in every perfection. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him eternity (al-qidam), which is the negation of prior non-existence, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated, and if He were originated, He would need an originator, and need would negate His self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that the beliefs derived from the attribute of self-sufficiency (al-istighna) are based on God's essential and eternal independence from all else. The author then derives the first attribute: necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud). The argument is that if God were contingent (ja'iz al-wujud), He would require a creator, which contradicts self-sufficiency. Since self-sufficiency is established by both reason and revelation (Quran 35:15), necessity of existence is proven. Reason's proof is the argument of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). Then the author inserts a note explaining the two names 'Jall' and 'Azza': 'Jall' means transcendence above any deficiency, and 'Azza' means uniqueness in all perfection. Finally, he derives eternity (al-qidam) from self-sufficiency. The proof is that if God were not eternal, He would be originated (hadith), and an originated being requires an originator, which again contradicts self-sufficiency. Thus, both necessary existence and eternity are necessary consequences of God's absolute self-sufficiency.

القيام بالنفس
جل
عز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary existence is a separate attribute unrelated to self-sufficiency.
Correction: The text shows that necessary existence is logically entailed by self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

A) Argument from eternity
B) Argument from self-subsistence
C) Argument from contingency
D) Argument from richness

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-sufficiency logically necessitates that His existence is necessary, not contingent.

Self-Sufficiency Proves Eternity
Sanusi derives eternity from self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's self-sufficiency also entails His eternity, meaning He has no prior non-existence.

Core Idea
If Allah were not eternal, He would need an originator, which contradicts His self-sufficiency.

Continuing from the previous proof, the author now derives another essential attribute from self-sufficiency: eternity (al-qidam). The logic parallels the proof for necessary existence: an originated being is contingent and requires a creator, which negates self-sufficiency. Thus, Allah must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the text for this lesson, focusing on the logical flow: if not eternal → originated → need originator → contradiction.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is: Self-sufficiency is established → if not eternal, then originated → need originator → contradiction → thus eternal.
Notice the similarity to the previous proof.
3
Reflect on the concept of eternity
Eternity here means no prior non-existence. Why is that important for tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العقائد التي تندرج تحت الاستغناء فهي غناه الذاتي الأبدي جل وعز عن كل ما سواه فاستغناؤه يوجب له تعالى وجوب الوجود لأنه لو كان جائز الوجود لافتقر إلى محدث والافتقار ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً قال الله تعالى ( يَتَأَيُّها النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْغَنِيُّ الْحَمِيدُ ﴾ [فاطر: ١٥) ، وأما العقل فهو برهان القيام بالنفس. ملاحظة : معنى جل هو تنزه عن كل نقص، ومعنى عزّ هو انفرد بكل كمال. و استغناؤه تعالى يوجب له القدم، أي انتفاء العدم السابق للوجود لأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، ولو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحدث، وينتفي عنه الغنى، ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
As for the beliefs that fall under self-sufficiency (al-istighna), they are His essential and eternal self-sufficiency, Majestic and Mighty, above everything other than Him. So His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud), because if He were contingent in existence (ja'iz al-wujud), He would need a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission. Allah said: 'O mankind, you are the poor toward Allah, and Allah is the Self-Sufficient, the Praiseworthy.' [Fatir:15]. As for reason, it is the proof of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). Note: The meaning of 'Jall' is His transcendence above every deficiency, and the meaning of 'Azza' is His uniqueness in every perfection. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him eternity (al-qidam), which is the negation of prior non-existence, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated, and if He were originated, He would need an originator, and need would negate His self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that the beliefs derived from the attribute of self-sufficiency (al-istighna) are based on God's essential and eternal independence from all else. The author then derives the first attribute: necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud). The argument is that if God were contingent (ja'iz al-wujud), He would require a creator, which contradicts self-sufficiency. Since self-sufficiency is established by both reason and revelation (Quran 35:15), necessity of existence is proven. Reason's proof is the argument of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs). Then the author inserts a note explaining the two names 'Jall' and 'Azza': 'Jall' means transcendence above any deficiency, and 'Azza' means uniqueness in all perfection. Finally, he derives eternity (al-qidam) from self-sufficiency. The proof is that if God were not eternal, He would be originated (hadith), and an originated being requires an originator, which again contradicts self-sufficiency. Thus, both necessary existence and eternity are necessary consequences of God's absolute self-sufficiency.

Key Terms
القدم
الاستغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is a separate attribute not derived from self-sufficiency.
Correction: The text explicitly derives eternity from self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the case if Allah were not eternal?
A) He would be self-sufficient
B) He would be originated
C) He would be necessary
D) He would be self-subsisting

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-sufficiency implies that He has no beginning; He is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's self-sufficiency proves He has no beginning, then what does your own constant need for others—from food to validation—say about your nature? How does this contrast make you see your complete dependence on Him, and how should that change how you live today?
?
The author states that self-sufficiency is proven 'by reason and transmission'—meaning both your intellect and revelation affirm it. Have you ever truly felt in your heart that Allah needs nothing from you, not even your worship? If He is absolutely self-sufficient, why do you still struggle to act as if your obedience is for your own benefit, not His? What would it look like to internalize this truth starting now?
?
The argument shows that need contradicts self-sufficiency. In what areas of your life do you pretend to be self-sufficient—relying on your own strength, wealth, or plans without turning to Allah? How does this lesson expose those as illusions, and what is one specific step you can take today to acknowledge your need for Him in that area?

The Dependence of All Things on God
Establishes that if any divine attribute is missing, no contingent thing can exist, and all things depend on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of any divine attribute would prevent the existence of contingent things, and why all contingent things necessarily depend on God.

Core Idea
Contingent existence depends entirely on God's attributes and power.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental principle: if any of God's attributes were lacking, nothing contingent could exist. He explains that contingency itself implies dependence on a cause, and that all contingent things are in need of God from the beginning and continuously. This principle is the foundation for proving God's unity and the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if any attribute were absent... then nothing contingent would exist.'
2
Understand the conditional argument
The author argues that if any attribute or its requirement were missing, contingent things could not exist, because the dependent cannot exist without its dependee. This shows that God's attributes are necessary for creation.
The 'cause of dependence' is contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth).
3
Grasp the necessity of universal dependence
The author then asserts that everything other than God depends on Him, both initially and perpetually. He challenges the idea that anything could be independent of God.
This sets up the arguments for unity and temporality that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو انتفى شيء من هذه الصفات أو مطلب من مطالبها وأحرى لو انتفى جميعها لما أمكن أن يوجد شيء من الحوادث، والعلة في ذلك استحالة وجود المتوقف بدون المتوقف عليه ، فلا يفتقر إليه شيء ، لأن علة الافتقار الإمكان والحدوث فلو انتفى شيء منها لما افتقر إليه شيء، كيف يتصور نفي الافتقار إليه تعالى وهو عز وجل يفتقر إليه كل ما سواه ابتداء بالإيجاد ودواماً بالإمداد. وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه جل وعلا يوجب له تعالى الوحدانية في الألوهية إذ لو كان معه ثان في الألوهية لما افتقر إليه شيء للزوم عجزهما حينئذ أي حين فرض وجودهما معا ويثبت العجز لهما سواء اتفقا أو اختلفا أو اقتسما ، لكن كونه لا يفتقر إليه شيء باطل لما سبق من وجوب افتقار كل ما سواه إليه ، كيف وهو جل وعلا الذي يفتقر إليه كل ما سواه ابتداء ودواما. ويؤخذ من افتقار كل ما سواه إليه حدوث العالم بأسره، إذ لو كان شيء منه قديماً لكان ذلك الشيء واجب الوجود ويكون مستغنياً عنه تعالى لوجوده وحصـــولـه ، وتحصيل الحاصل محال، إذ كونه تعالى لا يقبل العدم سابقاً ولا لاحقاً دليل على عدم افتقاره إلى مخصص، فيكون غنياً عنه تعالى، وهذا محال ، لأنه لو استغنى البعض لاستغنى الكل للتماثل وغنى كل الممكنات عنه تعالى محال، لوجوب افتقار كل ما سواه إليه، كيف يتصور عقلاً غنى شيء عنه تعالى وهو جل وعلا الذي يجب عقلاً أن يحتاج إليه كل ما سواه بدليل العقل والنقل.
And if any of these attributes or a requirement of them were absent, and more so if all were absent, it would not be possible for any contingent thing to exist. The cause of that is the impossibility of the dependent existing without that upon which it depends. So nothing would be in need of Him, because the cause of need is contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth). So if any of them were absent, nothing would be in need of Him. How can one imagine the negation of neediness towards Him, while He, majestic is He, is the one on whom everything other than Him depends initially by bringing into existence and perpetually by sustainment? And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him, exalted and majestic, necessitates for Him, exalted, unity in divinity (al-wahdaniyyah fi al-uluhiyyah), because if there were another with Him in divinity, nothing would be in need of Him due to the necessary incapacity of both then, i.e., when assuming their coexistence, and incapacity is established for both whether they agree or disagree or divide. But that nothing is in need of Him is false due to what preceded of the necessity of the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. How is that, while He, exalted, is the one on whom everything other than Him depends initially and perpetually? And from the dependence of everything other than Him, the temporal origination (huduth) of the entire world is inferred, because if any of it were pre-eternal, that thing would be necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) and would be independent of Him, exalted, due to its existence, and the attainment of what is already attained is impossible. Since He, exalted, does not admit non-existence (al-'adam) neither before nor after, that is evidence of His not being in need of a particularizer (mukhasis). So it would be independent of Him, exalted. And this is impossible, because if some were independent, all would be independent due to similarity, and the independence of all contingent things (al-mumkinat) from Him is impossible due to the necessity of the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. How can one rationally imagine independence of something from Him, while He, exalted, is the one whom everything other than Him must rationally need, by evidence of reason and transmitted revelation (al-'aql wa al-naql).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument establishing God's unity and the temporal origination of the world from the universal dependence of all contingent things on God. The author first reiterates that if any of God's attributes were missing, nothing contingent could exist, because contingency itself necessitates a cause. Then he argues that universal dependence entails God's unity in divinity: if there were two gods, nothing would depend on either, because both would be incapable. Since dependence is necessary, there is only one God. Then he infers the world's temporality: if any part of the world were eternal, it would be necessary of existence and thus independent of God; but independence of any contingent would imply independence of all, which is impossible because all contingent things need God. Therefore the entire world is temporally originated. This is a standard kalam argument from contingency to tawhid and huduth.

Key Terms
صفات (attributes)
حوادث (contingent events)
الافتقار (dependence)
الإمكان (contingency)
الحدوث (temporal origination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God could create even if He lacked some attributes, or that contingent things could exist without depending on God.
Correction: The author argues that contingency itself implies dependence on an external cause, and that cause must possess the attributes necessary for creation. Without a full set of attributes, nothing contingent could exist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause (علة) of a thing's dependence on God?
A) Its existence
B) Its contingency and temporal origination
C) Its need for a creator
D) The absence of attributes

Key Takeaway
All contingent things depend on God's complete attributes; without any of them, nothing dependent could exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence depends on God's attributes being fully present, why do you live as though you are autonomous and self-sufficient? What would change in your daily routine if you truly felt your dependency at every breath?
?
The passage says nothing would need God if He lacked any attribute. Examine your heart: do you ever act as if God's attributes are incomplete or as if you can manage without His constant sustainment? How would you reorient your life starting today to acknowledge that dependency?
?
You read that all contingent things—including you—depend on God 'initially by bringing into existence and perpetually by sustainment.' Are you actively thanking Him for each moment of existence, or have you grown numb to this reality? Write one concrete action you will take tonight to express your dependence on Him.

Unity and Temporal Origination from Dependence
Derives God's unity and the world's temporality from the universal dependence of all contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how universal dependence implies God's oneness in divinity and the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
Universal dependence proves both that God is one and that the world began in time.

Building on the established principle that all contingent things depend on God, the author now draws two major theological conclusions: first, that there can be only one God (since two would result in mutual incapacity), and second, that the entire world must be temporally originated (since any eternal part would be independent of God). This lesson unpacks these powerful derivations.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation below. Focus on the logical flow: from universal dependence to unity, then from universal dependence to temporality.
Notice how the author uses the same premise (dependence) for both conclusions.
2
Follow the unity argument
The author argues that if there were two gods, both would be incapable, and nothing would depend on them. Since dependence is necessary, there must be only one God.
The key point is that incapacity (عجز) would result from the assumption of two gods, regardless of whether they agree, disagree, or divide tasks.
3
Follow the temporality argument
If any part of the world were eternal, it would be necessary of existence and thus independent of God. But independence of any contingent would imply independence of all, which contradicts universal dependence. Therefore the entire world is temporally originated.
The author relies on the principle of similarity (تماثل): if one contingent could be independent, so could all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So nothing would be in need of Him, because the cause of need is contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth). So if any of them were absent, nothing would be in need of Him. But that nothing is in need of Him is false due to what preceded of the necessity of the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. Allah's Existence
intermediate
7 min
Contingents Depend on God in All States
Sanusi on the twofold dependence of contingents and refutation of autonomous creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent beings depend on God both for their initial existence and for their continued existence, and how this refutes false beliefs about creatures acting independently.

Core Idea
Everything in existence, even non-existence, points back to God.

Sanusi deepens our understanding of contingency by showing that dependence on God is not just a one-time event at creation. Whether a thing exists or does not exist, it remains entirely reliant on Allah. This passage also clarifies a common philosophical error: the mistaken notion that creatures can act independently through a power given to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two states of dependence
The passage explains dependence in both non-existence and existence. While dependence in non-existence is straightforward (need for creation), dependence in existence is argued through two possibilities regarding accidents.
3
Understand the accident argument
Examine the two views on whether accidents persist. Both lead to the same conclusion: the contingent needs God to sustain its existence, either by supplying new accidents or by preserving its existence.
The preferred view is that accidents persist, which still requires God's continuous power.
4
Grasp the remark on falsehood
The remark addresses two false beliefs: that a creature acts by its own nature, or that it acts by a power God placed in it. The first is refuted by dependence, the second by independence (since it would imply God needs a medium).
Many philosophers fell into this error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى كل حال أي في حالتي الوجود والعدم، فالممكن يفتقر إليه تعالى في الحالتين، أما في حالة العدم فلأنه يحتاج إليه تعالى في إيجاده، وأما في حالة الوجود فلأنا إن قلنا إن العرض لا يبقى زمانين افتقر الممكن إليه تعالى في إمداد ذاته بالأعراض التي لولا تعاقبها عليها لانعدمت ، وإن قلنا بأن العرض يبقى زمانين فأكثر وهو الراجح ، افتقر الممكن إليه تعالى أيضاً في دوام وجوده بناء على المختار : أن في العالم ما ليس بجرم ولا عرض كما يزعمه الفلاسفة، وعلى كل حال ابتداءً ودواماً. ملاحظة : إن قدرت أن شيئاً من الكائنات يؤثر بطبعه أي يحققه بذاته فإن بطلان هذا إنما يؤخذ من الافتقار وأما إن قدرته مؤثرا بقوة جعلها الله تعالى فيه فإنما يؤخذ بطلان هذا من الاستغناء. وقد تبع كثير الفلاسفة في هذا الاعتقاد.
And in every state, meaning in the two states of existence and non-existence, for the contingent (al-mumkin) depends on Him, Exalted is He, in both states. As for the state of non-existence, it needs Him, Exalted is He, to bring it into existence. As for the state of existence, if we say that the accident (al-'arad) does not persist for two moments, the contingent depends on Him, Exalted is He, for supplying its essence with accidents without which it would cease to exist. And if we say that the accident persists for two moments or more, which is the more correct view, the contingent also depends on Him, Exalted is He, for the continuation of its existence, based on the chosen view: that in the world there is what is neither a body nor an accident as the philosophers claim. And in every case, initially and perpetually. Remark: If you suppose that something among creatures affects by its nature, i.e., it realizes itself by itself, then the falsehood of this is taken from [the argument of] dependence. And if you suppose it to be an agent by a power God placed in it, then the falsehood of this is taken from [the argument of] independence. And many philosophers followed this belief.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage explains that the contingent being (al-mumkin) is dependent on God in both states: non-existence (where it needs God to bring it into existence) and existence (where it needs God to sustain its continued existence). The author discusses two views on the persistence of accidents: if accidents do not persist, then the contingent needs God to supply new accidents; if they do persist (the preferred view), the contingent still needs God for the continuation of its existence. This is based on the chosen view that there is nothing in the world that is self-subsisting. The passage then gives a remark: if you suppose a creature affects by its own nature, the refutation comes from the fact that it is dependent (on God). If you suppose it affects by a power God placed in it, the refutation comes from the fact that it would imply independence (from God). Many philosophers have followed this erroneous belief.

Key Terms
ممكن
عرض
استغناء
افتنقار
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A creature can act independently by a power given by God, implying it does not need direct divine causation for its effects.
Correction: Sanusi argues that such a belief is refuted by independence (الاستغناء), because it would imply the effect could be independent of God, which contradicts contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the argument that the contingent needs God for the continuation of its existence, even if accidents persist?
A) The fact that accidents do not persist
B) The chosen view that nothing in the world is self-subsisting
C) The philosophers' claim that there are non-bodies and non-accidents
D) The need for new accidents

Key Takeaway
Contingent beings depend on God not only for their creation but for every moment of their continued existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you act as if you can sustain yourself without God’s constant care? How does this passage challenge that assumption?
?
When you succeed or accomplish something, do you attribute it to your own power or to the power God gives you each moment? How does Sanusi’s remark about 'affecting by a power God placed in it' change how you view your own abilities?
?
If every moment of your existence depends entirely on God, what does that mean for your sense of security and pride? Can you truly say you rely on Him for the next breath?

The Possible Attribute of God
Sanusi defines the possible attribute of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for an action to be 'possible' for God and why this affirms His absolute free will.

Core Idea
God's possible actions show His unrestricted free will.

Before we examine proofs for God's existence, Sanusi establishes a conceptual foundation: what can be attributed to God? He defines the category of 'possible' (jāʾiz) — actions that God may do or leave without any constraint or compulsion. This clarifies that God's actions are not necessitated, preserving His freedom and majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Carefully read the Arabic sentence defining al-jāʾiz for God. Note the wording: 'فَفِعْلُ كُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ أَوْ تَرْكُهُ' — this is the entire definition.
The definition is concise and precise.
2
Understand the scope
Reflect on what is not included: necessary and impossible attributes are separate categories. The 'possible' covers everything that could be done or left undone.
God's actions are not limited by any external necessity.
3
Connect to free will
Consider why this definition matters: it affirms that God's actions are freely chosen. He is not compelled by any cause to act or refrain.
This distinguishes Islamic theology from philosophical necessitarianism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى فَفِعْلُ كُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ أَوْ تَرْكُهُ. أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ رَاجِحًا عَلَى الْآخَرِ بِلَا سَبَبٍ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ. وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ كَالْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ، وَمَا لَا يَخْلُو عَنِ الْحَوَادِثِ فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ. وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيُّرِهَا مِنْ عَدَمٍ إِلَى وُجُودٍ وَمِنْ وُجُودٍ إِلَى عَدَمٍ.
As for the possible in respect of Him, exalted, it is to do every possible or to leave it. As for the proof of His existence, exalted, it is the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. Because if it did not have a creator but rather came into existence by itself, it would necessitate that one of two equally possible states should be preponderant over the other without a cause, and that is impossible. The evidence for the temporality of the world is that it is not free from supervening accidents (aʿrāḍ ḥāditha) such as motion and rest; and whatever is not free from accidents is itself temporal. The evidence for the temporality of the accidents is the observation of their change from non‑existence to existence and from existence to non‑existence.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage first states that the possible (jāʾiz) in respect of God is that He may either do any possible thing or leave it – this emphasizes God's free will. Then it presents the proof for God's existence (burhān wujūdihi), which is the argument from the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al‑ʿālam). The argument proceeds: 1) If the world had no creator and came to be by itself, then among two equally possible states (e.g., existing at a certain time rather than another) one would be preferred without a cause, which is rationally impossible. Therefore the world must have a Creator. 2) To establish that the world is temporal, it argues that the world (bodies) is never free from accidents like motion and rest. These accidents are observed to change (from non‑existence to existence and vice versa), so they are temporal. 3) Whatever is never free from temporal things is itself temporal. Thus the world is temporal and needs a cause, which is God.

Key Terms
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'possible' implies God is imperfect or that He might change.
Correction: Rather, the 'possible' attribute underscores God's perfect freedom and power. He is not subject to any external compulsion; His actions are entirely by His own will.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the possible (jāʾiz) for God refer to?
A) Actions that God must do
B) Actions that God may do or leave
C) Actions that are impossible for God
D) Properties of God's essence

Key Takeaway
The possible attribute for God affirms that He freely chooses to do or leave any possible action, reflecting His absolute will and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disappointment—a prayer unanswered, a door closed—do you truly believe that God freely chose to leave that possible thing? Or does your heart accuse Him of being forced by circumstances? Answer honestly: will you now surrender to His jāʾiz will, not just in words but in your innermost peace?
?
If God could have left you uncreated, but instead chose to bring you into existence—what weight does that put on your response to Him today? Will you live this day as a deliberate act of gratitude, knowing He had every right not to create you?
?
Your life is filled with possibilities God has done or left undone. Is there an area where you are secretly insisting God 'must' act a certain way? Repent of that demand and ask: will you trust His free choice even when it costs you everything?

The Proof of God's Existence from Temporality
Sanusi presents the proof of God's existence from the temporality of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam argument from temporality (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) and why it rationally proves a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's dependence on changing accidents proves it is created and requires a Creator.

Having defined the possible attribute, Sanusi now presents the foundational proof for God's existence: the argument from temporality. This argument establishes that the world cannot be eternal because it is never free from temporal accidents. Therefore, it must have a creator who brought it into existence. The proof proceeds through careful logical steps that you will unpack in this lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the full proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى' to the end. Follow the logical flow: main claim, then evidence, then sub-evidence.
Notice the structure: burhān (proof) → reason → evidence for ḥudūth.
2
Understand the core argument
The proof states: if the world had no creator, it would have to cause itself. But self-causation would require one of two equally possible states to be preferred without cause, which is impossible. So a creator exists.
This is an argument by contradiction.
3
Examine the evidence for the world's temporality
Sanusi argues that the world is never free from accidents like motion and rest, which are observed to change. Whatever is never free from temporal things is itself temporal.
Accidents (aʿrāḍ) are properties that exist in bodies and are always changing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The world is temporal, so it could not exist eternally; it must have been brought into existence by a Creator — God.
This proof is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى فَفِعْلُ كُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ أَوْ تَرْكُهُ. أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ رَاجِحًا عَلَى الْآخَرِ بِلَا سَبَبٍ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ. وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو مِنَ الْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ كَالْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ، وَمَا لَا يَخْلُو عَنِ الْحَوَادِثِ فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ. وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيُّرِهَا مِنْ عَدَمٍ إِلَى وُجُودٍ وَمِنْ وُجُودٍ إِلَى عَدَمٍ.
As for the possible in respect of Him, exalted, it is to do every possible or to leave it. As for the proof of His existence, exalted, it is the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. Because if it did not have a creator but rather came into existence by itself, it would necessitate that one of two equally possible states should be preponderant over the other without a cause, and that is impossible. The evidence for the temporality of the world is that it is not free from supervening accidents (aʿrāḍ ḥāditha) such as motion and rest; and whatever is not free from accidents is itself temporal. The evidence for the temporality of the accidents is the observation of their change from non‑existence to existence and from existence to non‑existence.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage first states that the possible (jāʾiz) in respect of God is that He may either do any possible thing or leave it – this emphasizes God's free will. Then it presents the proof for God's existence (burhān wujūdihi), which is the argument from the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al‑ʿālam). The argument proceeds: 1) If the world had no creator and came to be by itself, then among two equally possible states (e.g., existing at a certain time rather than another) one would be preferred without a cause, which is rationally impossible. Therefore the world must have a Creator. 2) To establish that the world is temporal, it argues that the world (bodies) is never free from accidents like motion and rest. These accidents are observed to change (from non‑existence to existence and vice versa), so they are temporal. 3) Whatever is never free from temporal things is itself temporal. Thus the world is temporal and needs a cause, which is God.

Key Terms
برهان الحدوث
العالم
الأعراض
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that the world had a beginning in time (i.e., it was created at a specific moment).
Correction: The argument actually proves that the world is dependent on change and cannot be eternal; it does not necessarily specify a temporal beginning but rather that the world is contingent and requires a Creator.

Checkpoint

A) Because the world is too complex
B) Because it would require one equally possible state to be preferred without cause

D) Because accidents are eternal

Key Takeaway
The argument from temporality proves that the world cannot be eternal and self-caused; it must have a Creator, which is God.

?

Allah's Existence

6 min
Proof of God's Existence from the Temporality of the World
Sanusi establishes the temporality of the world and deduces a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world—proved by its change and inseparability from originated accidents—leads to the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's constant change proves it is originated, requiring a Creator.

Sanūsī begins the proof for God’s existence by focusing on the world itself. He argues that the world is clearly changing, and this change shows it cannot be self-caused. Instead, it must have an originator—God. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of this foundational kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic and then the English translation. Notice the concise style of the proof.
The passage uses technical terms like ḥudūth and muḥdith.
2
Understand the key premise: temporality of the world
The proof begins by asserting that the world is temporally originated. Sanūsī supports this by pointing to the world's constant change (taghayyur) and its inseparability from accidents that are clearly originated.
Accidents like motion and rest are observed to come and go.
3
Follow the logical structure
The argument is: if the world had no creator, it would have to cause itself. But then one of two equal states would be preferred without cause—an impossibility. Therefore, the world has a creator.
This uses the principle of 'preference without a preponderating factor' (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ).
4
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that God exists as the necessary originator of the originated world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُسَاوِيًا لِصَاحِبِهِ رَاجِحًا عَلَيْهِ بِلَا سَبَبٍ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ تَغَيُّرُهُ لِمُلَازَمَتِهِ لِلْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونٍ وَغَيْرِهِمَا، وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيُّرِهَا مِنْ عَدَمٍ إِلَى وُجُودٍ وَمِنْ وُجُودٍ إِلَى عَدَمٍ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيمًا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا فَيَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَيَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ أَوِ التَّسَلْسُلُ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ لَانْتَفَى عَنْهُ الْقِدَمُ لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ حِينَئِذٍ جَائِزًا لَا وَاجِبًا، وَالْجَائِزُ لَا يَكُونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلَّا حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَرِيبًا وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى وَبَقَائِهِ.
As for the proof of His existence, exalted, it is the temporality of the world, because if it had no originator but occurred by itself, then one of two equal things would necessarily be preferred over its counterpart without cause, which is impossible. The proof of the temporality of the world is its change due to its inseparability from originated accidents, such as motion and rest and others, and whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated. The proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. As for the proof of the necessity of eternity for Him, exalted, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus need an originator, leading to a circularity or infinite regress. As for the proof of the necessity of permanence for Him, exalted, because if non-existence could possibly affect Him, then eternity would be negated from Him, since His existence would then be possible not necessary, and whatever is possible only has originated existence, although the necessity of His eternity and permanence has already been established.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs: for God's existence, for His necessary eternity (qidam), and for His necessary permanence (baqā'). The first proof, for existence, is based on the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. The argument is that if the world had no creator but arose by itself, then one of two equally possible states would be preferred without cause, which is absurd. The world's temporality is proved by its constant change, which shows it is inseparable from accidents that are clearly originated (observed to come into and go out of existence). Since whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated, the world is originated and needs a creator. The second proof shows that God must be eternal: if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus require an originator, leading to either an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal. The third proof shows that God must be permanent (everlasting): if it were possible for Him to cease to exist, then His existence would be possible (jā’iz) rather than necessary, and whatever has possible existence is originated. But He is eternal, so He must also be permanent. The conclusion is that the necessity of His eternity and permanence is established.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
المحدث
الأعراض الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of God's existence is merely cosmological, based on the world having a beginning in time.
Correction: The proof actually relies on the impossibility of preference without cause (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ) and the world's contingency shown by its change.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise in the proof of God's existence?
A) The world has a beginning in time
B) The world is composed of atoms
C) The world is inseparable from originated accidents
D) The world shows design

Key Takeaway
The world's constant change proves it is originated and requires an eternal Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's temporality proves it must have a Creator, what does your own temporality—your constant change from one state to another—say about your need for God? Are you aware that every breath is a new creation? How should this awareness change your prayer today?
?
The proof shows that without a cause, nothing can come to be. Reflect on the causes of your current state of faith: what has brought you to this moment? Are you relying on the same Cause that brought the world into existence, or are you trusting in secondary causes that themselves are originated? What is the one change you need to make to align your reliance more directly on the Creator?
?
Sanusi argues that one of two equal possibilities cannot become preferred without a cause. Consider a decision you are facing where both options seem equally valid. Are you seeking a cause (God's guidance) to prefer one, or are you relying on your own whim? How does this argument challenge the way you make choices today?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
Proof of God's Eternity (Qidam)
Sanusi proves that God must be eternal to avoid circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be eternal: if He were originated, it would lead to an impossible circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If God were originated, He would need a creator, leading to an infinite chain—an impossibility.

After establishing God's existence, Sanūsī turns to His attribute of eternity (qidam). He argues that if God were not eternal, He would be originated and thus dependent on another, which would set off an unacceptable chain of causes. This lesson explains the brief but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for eternity
Read the Arabic and translation for this short proof. Identify the conditional structure: 'if not eternal, then originated, then need originator, leading to circularity or regress.'
The proof is concise; note the terms dawr (circularity) and tasalsul (infinite regress).
2
Understand why circularity and regress are impossible
Circularity (A depends on B, B depends on A) is logically impossible. Infinite regress (an endless chain of dependencies) is also impossible in kalam because it would never provide a true cause.
This is a standard argument for a First Cause.
3
Connect to God's attribute of eternity
Since the alternative (being originated) leads to impossibility, God must be eternal—He has no beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُسَاوِيًا لِصَاحِبِهِ رَاجِحًا عَلَيْهِ بِلَا سَبَبٍ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ تَغَيُّرُهُ لِمُلَازَمَتِهِ لِلْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونٍ وَغَيْرِهِمَا، وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيُّرِهَا مِنْ عَدَمٍ إِلَى وُجُودٍ وَمِنْ وُجُودٍ إِلَى عَدَمٍ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيمًا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا فَيَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَيَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ أَوِ التَّسَلْسُلُ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ لَانْتَفَى عَنْهُ الْقِدَمُ لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ حِينَئِذٍ جَائِزًا لَا وَاجِبًا، وَالْجَائِزُ لَا يَكُونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلَّا حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَرِيبًا وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى وَبَقَائِهِ.
As for the proof of His existence, exalted, it is the temporality of the world, because if it had no originator but occurred by itself, then one of two equal things would necessarily be preferred over its counterpart without cause, which is impossible. The proof of the temporality of the world is its change due to its inseparability from originated accidents, such as motion and rest and others, and whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated. The proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. As for the proof of the necessity of eternity for Him, exalted, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus need an originator, leading to a circularity or infinite regress. As for the proof of the necessity of permanence for Him, exalted, because if non-existence could possibly affect Him, then eternity would be negated from Him, since His existence would then be possible not necessary, and whatever is possible only has originated existence, although the necessity of His eternity and permanence has already been established.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs: for God's existence, for His necessary eternity (qidam), and for His necessary permanence (baqā'). The first proof, for existence, is based on the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. The argument is that if the world had no creator but arose by itself, then one of two equally possible states would be preferred without cause, which is absurd. The world's temporality is proved by its constant change, which shows it is inseparable from accidents that are clearly originated (observed to come into and go out of existence). Since whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated, the world is originated and needs a creator. The second proof shows that God must be eternal: if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus require an originator, leading to either an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal. The third proof shows that God must be permanent (everlasting): if it were possible for Him to cease to exist, then His existence would be possible (jā’iz) rather than necessary, and whatever has possible existence is originated. But He is eternal, so He must also be permanent. The conclusion is that the necessity of His eternity and permanence is established.

Key Terms
القدم
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the proof of eternity argue would happen if God were not eternal?
A) He would be non-existent
B) He would need an originator, causing circularity or regress
C) He would be composed of parts
D) He would be imperfect

Key Takeaway
God must be eternal because any alternative leads to an impossible chain of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not eternal, He would need a creator. Do you truly live as if your very existence depends on an Eternal Being every moment, or do you subtly rely on created things as your ultimate foundation?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible. Yet we often act as if our desires, worries, or plans go on forever. How does realizing that only an Eternal God can end the chain change what you should prioritize today?
?
Sanusi argues that God must be eternal because any alternative leads to an absurdity. Have you ever considered that your own reasoning about God's existence might be based on assumptions that lead to contradictions? Are you willing to accept the logical conclusion that only an Eternal Being can be your Creator?

Proof of God's Permanence (Baqā')
Sanusi proves God's permanence, linking it to His necessary existence and eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be permanent: if possible non-existence were allowed, His existence would be contingent and thus originated, contradicting His established eternity.

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He wouldn't be necessary—and what is not necessary cannot be eternal.

The final proof in this series establishes God's permanence (baqā'). Sanūsī argues that if it were possible for God to cease to exist, then His existence would be merely possible (jā’iz) rather than necessary. But a merely possible existence is originated, which contradicts His proven eternity. This lesson unpacks this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for permanence
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical flow: if non-existence possible, then existence is possible, not necessary; what is possible is originated; but God is eternal, so He cannot be originated.
The proof relies on the categories of necessary (wājib) and possible (jā’iz).
2
Understand the link between necessity and permanence
A necessary being cannot possibly not exist. If it were possible for God to cease, He would not be necessary. Since the earlier proof established God is eternal (and thus necessary), permanence follows.
Sanūsī says 'the necessity of His eternity and permanence has already been established'—pointing back to the previous proof.
3
Recognize the interdependence of attributes
Eternity and permanence are two sides of the same coin: God is without beginning and without end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُودِهِ تَعَالَى فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مُحْدِثٌ بَلْ حَدَثَ بِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُسَاوِيًا لِصَاحِبِهِ رَاجِحًا عَلَيْهِ بِلَا سَبَبٍ وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ تَغَيُّرُهُ لِمُلَازَمَتِهِ لِلْأَعْرَاضِ الْحَادِثَةِ مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونٍ وَغَيْرِهِمَا، وَمُلَازِمُ الْحَادِثِ حَادِثٌ، وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيُّرِهَا مِنْ عَدَمٍ إِلَى وُجُودٍ وَمِنْ وُجُودٍ إِلَى عَدَمٍ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيمًا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا فَيَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَيَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ أَوِ التَّسَلْسُلُ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ لَانْتَفَى عَنْهُ الْقِدَمُ لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ حِينَئِذٍ جَائِزًا لَا وَاجِبًا، وَالْجَائِزُ لَا يَكُونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلَّا حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَرِيبًا وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى وَبَقَائِهِ.
As for the proof of His existence, exalted, it is the temporality of the world, because if it had no originator but occurred by itself, then one of two equal things would necessarily be preferred over its counterpart without cause, which is impossible. The proof of the temporality of the world is its change due to its inseparability from originated accidents, such as motion and rest and others, and whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated. The proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. As for the proof of the necessity of eternity for Him, exalted, because if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus need an originator, leading to a circularity or infinite regress. As for the proof of the necessity of permanence for Him, exalted, because if non-existence could possibly affect Him, then eternity would be negated from Him, since His existence would then be possible not necessary, and whatever is possible only has originated existence, although the necessity of His eternity and permanence has already been established.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs: for God's existence, for His necessary eternity (qidam), and for His necessary permanence (baqā'). The first proof, for existence, is based on the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. The argument is that if the world had no creator but arose by itself, then one of two equally possible states would be preferred without cause, which is absurd. The world's temporality is proved by its constant change, which shows it is inseparable from accidents that are clearly originated (observed to come into and go out of existence). Since whatever is inseparable from an originated thing is itself originated, the world is originated and needs a creator. The second proof shows that God must be eternal: if He were not eternal, He would be originated and thus require an originator, leading to either an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal. The third proof shows that God must be permanent (everlasting): if it were possible for Him to cease to exist, then His existence would be possible (jā’iz) rather than necessary, and whatever has possible existence is originated. But He is eternal, so He must also be permanent. The conclusion is that the necessity of His eternity and permanence is established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's permanence is absolutely necessary, then nothing in your life that fades—health, relationships, youth—can be your ultimate anchor. Where do you actually place your trust when you are afraid of losing something? Let this question sit until you feel the weight of an honest answer.
?
Reflect on a moment when you doubted that God would 'still be there' after a trial. The proof shows that even the possibility of His non-existence is absurd. How does this logical certainty confront the anxiety in your heart? Will you let it transform your fear into reliance?
?
You claim to believe in a God who cannot cease to exist. Yet do your daily choices—where you seek comfort, approval, and control—reflect that permanence? Name one area where you live as if God could 'fail' you, and commit to a specific change this week that acknowledges His everlasting reality.

Rational Proofs for Essential Divine Attributes
Sanusi enumerates three rational proofs for essential divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for God's pre-eternity, oneness, and essential attributes as presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Each essential divine attribute is proven by the impossibility of creation without it.

Sanusi lays out three concise rational proofs that establish the necessary attributes of God. These proofs are foundational in Islamic theology, showing that the existence of the world depends on God being eternal, one, and possessing power, will, knowledge, and life. The passage is compact, but each statement contains a complete logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning. Notice that the first proof for pre-eternity is stated without elaboration.
The first proof may require referring back to earlier discussions; here it is affirmed as a foundation.
2
Identify the three separate proofs
The passage contains three distinct proofs: for pre-eternity/everlastingness, for oneness, and for the four attributes (power, will, knowledge, life). Note how each is introduced with 'وأما'.
Each proof follows a similar logical structure: if the attribute were absent, the world could not exist.
3
Understand the logical chain for oneness
The proof for oneness says: if God were not one (i.e., if there were multiple gods), then each would be incapable (because of mutual conflict), and consequently nothing would exist. Ask yourself: why does multiplicity imply incapability?
The incapability arises because each would depend on the other for existence or action.
4
Understand the logical chain for the attributes
The proof for the four attributes states that if any one of them were missing, temporal events (the entire world) would not exist. Consider how each attribute (power, will, knowledge, life) is necessary for creation.
The teacher's commentary explains this dependency: a creator must have power to act, will to choose, knowledge to design, and life to be an agent.
5
Reflect on the absence of reasoning for pre-eternity
Sanusi states the proof for pre-eternity and everlastingness but does not give the reasoning here. Think about why this attribute is considered necessary: if God were not pre-eternal, He would be caused, and if not everlasting, He would cease to exist—both contradict His being the Creator of all else.
The commentary notes that the reasoning may be implied from earlier sections or considered self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْبُرْهَانُ عَلَى وُجُوبِ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى وَبَقَائِهِ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةِ لَهُ تَعَالَى، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وَاحِداً لَزِمَ أَنْ لَا يُوجَدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ لِكَوْنِهِ عَاجِزاً حِينَئِذٍ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ أَوْصَافِهِ تَعَالَى بِالْقُدْرَةِ وَالْإِرَادَةِ وَالْعِلْمِ وَالْحَيَاةِ، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ.
The proof for the necessity of His, exalted is He, pre-eternity and everlastingness. And as for the proof for the necessity of oneness for Him, exalted is He, it is because if He were not one, it would necessarily follow that nothing of the world would exist due to His being incapable at that time. And as for the proof for the necessity of His attributes, exalted is He, with power, will, knowledge, and life, it is because if any of them were absent, nothing of temporal events would exist.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage provides rational proofs for the essential attributes of God. It begins by stating the proof for His pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'), though the specific reasoning is not elaborated here. Then it gives the proof for oneness (wahdaniyya): if God were not one (i.e., if there were multiple gods), each would be incapable by virtue of the other's existence, and thus nothing could be created. Finally, it presents the proof for the attributes of power (qudra), will (irada), knowledge ('ilm), and life (hayat): if any of these were absent, the existence of temporal events (hawadith) would be impossible, because the world's existence depends on a powerful, willing, knowing, and living agent.

Key Terms
قِدَم
بَقَاء
وَحْدَانِيَّة
قُدْرَة
إِرَادَة
عِلْم
حَيَاة
حَوَادِث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof for oneness assumes multiple gods are possible, and then proves they are not possible. Actually, the proof shows that if multiple gods existed, they would be incapable, so the very assumption leads to an absurdity (non-existence of the world), thus proving oneness.
Correction: The proof does not assume the possibility of multiple gods; it demonstrates that multiplicity is logically impossible because it would entail incapability and the non-existence of the world.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would necessarily follow if God were not one?
A) Nothing of the world would exist due to His incapability.
B) The world would still exist but with multiple creators.
C) His attributes would be incomplete.
D) He would need a partner.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the world necessitates that its Creator is eternal, one, and possesses power, will, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence of every temporal event—including this very moment—depends on God possessing power, will, knowledge, and life, would you treat any event as random or meaningless? What would it look like to consciously attribute every event of your day to a God who is perfectly powerful, knowing, and alive?
?
The proof for oneness shows that if God were not one, nothing would exist—including you. But do you sometimes act as if other 'forces' in your life (money, reputation, people's opinions) have independent power to create your happiness or misery? Which of these false 'gods' do you need to renounce today, and what concrete step can you take to trust in the One alone?
?
Sanusi says that if any of the attributes—power, will, knowledge, life—were absent, there would be no temporal events. The most recent event in your life—a success, a failure, a conversation—could it have occurred if God lacked even one of these? How does that change your attitude toward that event, and toward the One who brought it about?

The Meaning of 'La Ilaha Illa Allah'
Sanusi defines the Shahada through the concepts of self-sufficiency and dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the declaration of faith as defined by Sanusi: God's absolute self-sufficiency and the world's total dependence.

Core Idea
Divinity means total self-sufficiency and absolute dependence.

The Shahada is not just a phrase; it carries a precise theological meaning. Sanusi unpacks it by defining the essence of divinity (uluhiyya) as two inseparable aspects: God's self-sufficiency from all else, and everything else's dependence on Him. This lesson establishes that foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Notice the structure: Sanusi first states that the Shahada gathers all beliefs, then defines divinity.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two aspects of divinity
Find where Sanusi defines self-sufficiency (istighna') and dependence (iftiqar). Understand how these two components are the core of uluhiyya.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'except Allah'
The phrase 'except Allah' limits self-sufficiency and dependence to God alone. Consider what this implies about everything else.
This shows the shahada is both negation and affirmation.
